<meta name="DC.Title" content="Acidophilic green algal genome provides insights into adaptation to an acidic environment" />
<meta name="DC.Identifier" content="10.1073/pnas.1707072114" />

<meta name="DC.Description" content="Extremely acidic environments are scattered worldwide, and their ecosystems are supported by acidophilic microalgae as primary producers. To understand how acidophilic algae evolved from their respective neutrophilic ancestors, we determined the draft genome sequence of the acidophilic green alga Chlamydomonas eustigma and performed comparative genome analyses between C . eustigma and its neutrophilic relative Chlamydomonas reinhardtii . The results suggest that higher expression of heat-shock proteins and H+-ATPase, loss of some metabolic pathways that acidify cytosol, and acquisition of metal-detoxifying genes by horizontal gene transfer have played important roles in the adaptation to acidic environments. These features are also found in other acidophilic green and red algae, suggesting the existence of common mechanisms in the adaptation to acidic environments." />

<meta name="citation_title" content="Acidophilic green algal genome provides insights into adaptation to an acidic environment" />
<meta name="citation_abstract" lang="en" content="&lt;p&gt;Some microalgae are adapted to extremely acidic environments in which toxic metals are present at high levels. However, little is known about how acidophilic algae evolved from their respective neutrophilic ancestors by adapting to particular acidic environments. To gain insights into this issue, we determined the draft genome sequence of the acidophilic green alga &lt;i&gt;Chlamydomonas eustigma&lt;/i&gt; and performed comparative genome and transcriptome analyses between &lt;i&gt;C&lt;/i&gt;. &lt;i&gt;eustigma&lt;/i&gt; and its neutrophilic relative &lt;i&gt;Chlamydomonas reinhardtii&lt;/i&gt;. The results revealed the following features in &lt;i&gt;C&lt;/i&gt;. &lt;i&gt;eustigma&lt;/i&gt; that probably contributed to the adaptation to an acidic environment. Genes encoding heat-shock proteins and plasma membrane H&lt;sup&gt;+&lt;/sup&gt;-ATPase are highly expressed in &lt;i&gt;C&lt;/i&gt;. &lt;i&gt;eustigma&lt;/i&gt;. This species has also lost fermentation pathways that acidify the cytosol and has acquired an energy shuttle and buffering system and arsenic detoxification genes through horizontal gene transfer. Moreover, the arsenic detoxification genes have been multiplied in the genome. These features have also been found in other acidophilic green and red algae, suggesting the existence of common mechanisms in the adaptation to acidic environments.&lt;/p&gt;" />
<meta name="citation_abstract" lang="en" scheme="executive-summary" content="&lt;h3&gt;Significance&lt;/h3&gt;
&lt;p&gt;Extremely acidic environments are scattered worldwide, and their ecosystems are supported by acidophilic microalgae as primary producers. To understand how acidophilic algae evolved from their respective neutrophilic ancestors, we determined the draft genome sequence of the acidophilic green alga &lt;i&gt;Chlamydomonas eustigma&lt;/i&gt; and performed comparative genome analyses between &lt;i&gt;C&lt;/i&gt;. &lt;i&gt;eustigma&lt;/i&gt; and its neutrophilic relative &lt;i&gt;Chlamydomonas reinhardtii&lt;/i&gt;. The results suggest that higher expression of heat-shock proteins and H&lt;sup&gt;+&lt;/sup&gt;-ATPase, loss of some metabolic pathways that acidify cytosol, and acquisition of metal-detoxifying genes by horizontal gene transfer have played important roles in the adaptation to acidic environments. These features are also found in other acidophilic green and red algae, suggesting the existence of common mechanisms in the adaptation to acidic environments.&lt;/p&gt;" />

<meta name="twitter:title" content="Acidophilic green algal genome provides insights into adaptation to an acidic environment" />
<meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image" />
<meta name="twitter:image" content="https://www.pnas.org/content/114/39/E8304/F1.large.jpg" />
<meta name="twitter:description" content="Extremely acidic environments are scattered worldwide, and their ecosystems are supported by acidophilic microalgae as primary producers. To understand how acidophilic algae evolved from their respective neutrophilic ancestors, we determined the draft genome sequence of the acidophilic green alga Chlamydomonas eustigma and performed comparative genome analyses between C . eustigma and its neutrophilic relative Chlamydomonas reinhardtii . The results suggest that higher expression of heat-shock proteins and H+-ATPase, loss of some metabolic pathways that acidify cytosol, and acquisition of metal-detoxifying genes by horizontal gene transfer have played important roles in the adaptation to acidic environments. These features are also found in other acidophilic green and red algae, suggesting the existence of common mechanisms in the adaptation to acidic environments." />

<meta name="og-description" property="og:description" content="Extremely acidic environments are scattered worldwide, and their ecosystems are supported by acidophilic microalgae as primary producers. To understand how acidophilic algae evolved from their respective neutrophilic ancestors, we determined the draft genome sequence of the acidophilic green alga Chlamydomonas eustigma and performed comparative genome analyses between C . eustigma and its neutrophilic relative Chlamydomonas reinhardtii . The results suggest that higher expression of heat-shock proteins and H+-ATPase, loss of some metabolic pathways that acidify cytosol, and acquisition of metal-detoxifying genes by horizontal gene transfer have played important roles in the adaptation to acidic environments. These features are also found in other acidophilic green and red algae, suggesting the existence of common mechanisms in the adaptation to acidic environments." />

      <div class="zone zone-content row">

          <div class="region region-content col-narrow-22 col-narrow-offset-1">
    <div id="block-panels-mini-whats-new-in" class="block block-panels-mini">

    
  <div class="content">
    <div class="panel-display panel-1col clearfix" id="mini-panel-whats_new_in">
  <div class="panel-panel panel-col">
    <div><div class="panel-pane pane-custom pane-4 whats-new-in-title" >
  
      
  
  <div class="pane-content">
    <h2>New&nbsp;Research&nbsp;In</h2>
  </div>

  
  </div>
<div class="panel-pane pane-snippet pane-pnas-physical-sciences wni-dropdown" >
  
        <h3 class="pane-title"><span class="pane-title-text">Physical Sciences</span></h3>
    
  
  <div class="pane-content">
    <div class="pnas-physical-sciences" id="pnas-physical-sciences">
  
      
  <div class="snippet-content">
    <h4>Featured Portals</h4>

<ul class="menu">
	<li><a href="http://physics.pnas.org/">Physics</a></li>
	<li><a href="http://chemistry.pnas.org/">Chemistry</a></li>
	<li><a href="http://sustainability.pnas.org/">Sustainability Science</a></li>
</ul>

<h4>Articles by Topic</h4>

<ul class="menu">
	<li><a href="/content/by/section/Applied%20Mathematics">Applied Mathematics</a></li>
	<li><a href="/content/by/section/Applied%20Physical%20Sciences">Applied Physical Sciences</a></li>
	<li><a href="/content/by/section/Astronomy">Astronomy</a></li>
	<li><a href="/content/by/section/Computer%20Sciences">Computer Sciences</a></li>
	<li><a href="/content/by/section/Earth,%20Atmospheric,%20and%20Planetary%20Sciences">Earth, Atmospheric, and Planetary Sciences</a></li>
	<li><a href="/content/by/section/Engineering">Engineering</a></li>
	<li><a href="/content/by/section/Environmental%20Sciences">Environmental Sciences</a></li>

<div class="panel-separator"></div><div class="panel-pane pane-highwire-panel-tabs-container" >
  
      
  
  <div class="pane-content">
    <div data-panels-ajax-tab-preloaded="jnl_pnas_tab_art" id="panels-ajax-tab-container-highwire_article_tabs" class="panels-ajax-tab-container"><div class="panels-ajax-tab-loading" style ="display:none"><img class="loading" src="https://www.pnas.org/sites/all/modules/contrib/panels_ajax_tab/images/loading.gif" alt="Loading" title="Loading" /></div><div class="panels-ajax-tab-wrap-jnl_pnas_tab_art"><div class="panel-display panel-1col clearfix" >
  <div class="panel-panel panel-col">
    <div><div class="panel-pane pane-highwire-markup" >
  
      
  
  <div class="pane-content">
    <div class="highwire-markup"><div xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" id="content-block-markup" data-highwire-cite-ref-tooltip-instance="highwire_reflinks_tooltip" xmlns:xhtml="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml"><div class="article fulltext-view "><span class="highwire-journal-article-marker-start"></span><div class="executive-summary"><h2 class="">Significance</h2><p id="p-3">Extremely acidic environments are scattered worldwide, and their ecosystems are supported by acidophilic microalgae as primary producers. To understand how acidophilic algae evolved from their respective neutrophilic ancestors, we determined the draft genome sequence of the acidophilic green alga <em>Chlamydomonas eustigma</em> and performed comparative genome analyses between <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em> and its neutrophilic relative <em>Chlamydomonas reinhardtii</em>. The results suggest that higher expression of heat-shock proteins and H<sup>+</sup>-ATPase, loss of some metabolic pathways that acidify cytosol, and acquisition of metal-detoxifying genes by horizontal gene transfer have played important roles in the adaptation to acidic environments. These features are also found in other acidophilic green and red algae, suggesting the existence of common mechanisms in the adaptation to acidic environments.</p></div><div class="section abstract" id="abstract-2"><h2>Abstract</h2><p id="p-4">Some microalgae are adapted to extremely acidic environments in which toxic metals are present at high levels. However, little is known about how acidophilic algae evolved from their respective neutrophilic ancestors by adapting to particular acidic environments. To gain insights into this issue, we determined the draft genome sequence of the acidophilic green alga <em>Chlamydomonas eustigma</em> and performed comparative genome and transcriptome analyses between <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em> and its neutrophilic relative <em>Chlamydomonas reinhardtii</em>. The results revealed the following features in <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em> that probably contributed to the adaptation to an acidic environment. Genes encoding heat-shock proteins and plasma membrane H<sup>+</sup>-ATPase are highly expressed in <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em>. This species has also lost fermentation pathways that acidify the cytosol and has acquired an energy shuttle and buffering system and arsenic detoxification genes through horizontal gene transfer. Moreover, the arsenic detoxification genes have been multiplied in the genome. These features have also been found in other acidophilic green and red algae, suggesting the existence of common mechanisms in the adaptation to acidic environments.</p></div><ul class="kwd-group"><li class="kwd"><a href="/keyword/environmental-adaptation" class="hw-term hw-article-keyword hw-article-keyword-environmental-adaptation" rel="nofollow">environmental adaptation</a></li><li class="kwd"><a href="/keyword/acidic-environment" class="hw-term hw-article-keyword hw-article-keyword-acidic-environment" rel="nofollow">acidic environment</a></li><li class="kwd"><a href="/keyword/acidophilic-alga" class="hw-term hw-article-keyword hw-article-keyword-acidophilic-alga" rel="nofollow">acidophilic alga</a></li><li class="kwd"><a href="/keyword/comparative-genomics" class="hw-term hw-article-keyword hw-article-keyword-comparative-genomics" rel="nofollow">comparative genomics</a></li><li class="kwd"><a href="/keyword/comparative-transcriptomics" class="hw-term hw-article-keyword hw-article-keyword-comparative-transcriptomics" rel="nofollow">comparative transcriptomics</a></li></ul><p id="p-5" class="flushleft">Several eukaryotic microalgae have been identified in acidic environments (pH &lt;4.0) such as acid mine drainage (AMD) and geothermal hot springs (<a id="xref-ref-1-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-1">1</a>). In this pH range, cyanobacteria are not present, and only acidophilic eukaryotic phototrophs are capable of photosynthesis (<a id="xref-fig-1-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F1">Fig. 1</a>) (<a id="xref-ref-2-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-2">2</a>, <a id="xref-ref-3-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-3">3</a>). The extremely low pH of these waters is due to the dissolution and oxidation of sulfur that is exposed to water and oxygen and produces sulfuric acid (<a id="xref-ref-4-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-4">4</a>). The low pH facilitates metal solubility in water; therefore, acidic waters tend to have high concentrations of metals (<a id="xref-ref-5-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-5">5</a>). Thus, acidophilic eukaryotic algae usually possess the ability to cope with toxic heavy metals in addition to low pH, both of which are lethal to most eukaryotes (<a id="xref-ref-2-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-2">2</a>). Acidophilic algae are distributed throughout different branches of the eukaryotes, such as in red and green algae, stramenopiles, and euglenids. In most cases, neutrophilic relatives have been identified, suggesting that acidophilic algae evolved from their respective neutrophilic ancestors multiple times independently (<a id="xref-ref-6-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-6">6</a>). However, it is largely unknown how several lineages of algae have successfully adapted to their acidic environments.</p><div id="F1" class="fig pos-float type-featured  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/114/39/E8304/F1.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Habitat, taxonomic position, and physiological features of the acidophilic green alga C. eustigma. (A) The algae inhabiting AMD in Yokote, Nagano Prefecture, Japan, and confirmation of the existence of C. eustigma. Algae were found predominantly in association with acidophilic mosses. (Scale bars: 10 &#x3BC;m.) (B) pH, temperature, and concentrations of some ions in the AMD. (C) Cells of C. eustigma NIES-2499 (Left) and C. reinhardtii 137c mt+ (Right). (Scale bar: 10 &#xB5;m.) (D) A phylogenetic tree of green and red algae based on the concatenated datasets (21 taxa, 11,367 sites) of five chloroplast protein-coding genes (atpB, psaA, psaB, psbC, and rbcL) and chloroplast ribosomal DNA sequence (16S and 23S). The maximum likelihood (ML) (RaxML 8.0.0) and Bayesian (MrBayes 3.2.6) analyses were calculated under separate model conditions. Bootstrap values (BP) &gt;50% obtained by ML and Bayesian posterior probabilities (BI) &gt;0.95 obtained by Bayesian analysis (MrBayes 3.2.6) are shown above the branches. The branch lengths reflect the evolutionary distances indicated by the scale bar. Filled red circles on the right indicate organisms for which genomes have been sequenced thus far. (E) C. eustigma and C. reinhardtii were cultured for 1 d in the same photoautotrophic medium at a series of pH. (F) Growth rates of C. eustigma and C. reinhardtii based on the increase in the cell number at the indicated pH. <li class="new-tab"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/114/39/E8304/F1.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab" target="_blank" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Open in new tab</a></li>
<li class="download-ppt last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/27928" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download powerpoint</a></li>
</ul></div><div class="fig-caption" xmlns:xhtml="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml"><span class="fig-label">Fig. 1.</span> <p id="p-6" class="first-child">Habitat, taxonomic position, and physiological features of the acidophilic green alga <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em>. (<em>A</em>) The algae inhabiting AMD in Yokote, Nagano Prefecture, Japan, and confirmation of the existence of <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em>. Algae were found predominantly in association with acidophilic mosses. (Scale bars: 10 μm.) (<em>B</em>) pH, temperature, and concentrations of some ions in the AMD. (<em>C</em>) Cells of <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em> NIES-2499 (<em>Left</em>) and <em>C</em>. <em>reinhardtii</em> 137c mt+ (<em>Right</em>). (Scale bar: 10 µm.) (<em>D</em>) A phylogenetic tree of green and red algae based on the concatenated datasets (21 taxa, 11,367 sites) of five chloroplast protein-coding genes (<em>atpB</em>, <em>psaA</em>, <em>psaB</em>, <em>psbC</em>, and <em>rbcL</em>) and chloroplast ribosomal DNA sequence (16S and 23S). The maximum likelihood (ML) (RaxML 8.0.0) and Bayesian (MrBayes 3.2.6) analyses were calculated under separate model conditions. Bootstrap values (BP) &gt;50% obtained by ML and Bayesian posterior probabilities (BI) &gt;0.95 obtained by Bayesian analysis (MrBayes 3.2.6) are shown above the branches. The branch lengths reflect the evolutionary distances indicated by the scale bar. Filled red circles on the right indicate organisms for which genomes have been sequenced thus far. (<em>E</em>) <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em> and <em>C</em>. <em>reinhardtii</em> were cultured for 1 d in the same photoautotrophic medium at a series of pH. (<em>F</em>) Growth rates of <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em> and <em>C</em>. <em>reinhardtii</em> based on the increase in the cell number at the indicated pH. The error bars represent the SD of three biological replicates.</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div><p id="p-7">Thus, far, the genomes of three related thermo-acidophilic red algae, <em>Cyanidioschyzon merolae</em> (<a id="xref-ref-7-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-7">7</a>), <em>Galdieria sulphuraria</em> (<a id="xref-ref-8-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-8">8</a>), and <em>Galdieria phlegrea</em> (<a id="xref-ref-9-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-9">9</a>), have been sequenced (all belong to the cyanidialean red algae, which inhabit sulfuric hot springs worldwide and grow optimally at 40–45 °C and pH 2–3). Genomic analyses showed that horizontal gene transfer (HGT) from environmental prokaryotes, the expansion of gene families, and the loss of genes have probably played important roles in the adaptation of Cyanidiales to acidic and high-temperature environments (<a id="xref-ref-8-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-8">8</a>). Through HGT, cyanidialean red algae acquired arsenical-resistance efflux pumps that biotransform arsenic and archaeal ATPases, which probably contribute to the algal heat tolerance (<a id="xref-ref-8-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-8">8</a>). In addition, the reduction in the number of genes encoding voltage-gated ion channels and the expansion of chloride channel and chloride carrier/channel families in the genome has probably contributed to the algal acid tolerance (<a id="xref-ref-8-4" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-8">8</a>). Likewise, a study in the acidophilic green alga <em>Chlamydomonas acidophila</em> showed that phytochelatin synthase genes of bacterial HGT origin played an important role in the tolerance to cadmium (<a id="xref-ref-10-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-10">10</a>).</p><p id="p-8">However, the genomes of acidophilic algae other than cyanidialean red algae have not been sequenced. The green and red algae diverged relatively soon after the emergence of primitive eukaryotic algae (<a id="xref-ref-11-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-11">11</a>). In addition, comparisons with neutrophilic relatives are feasible in the case of acidophilic green algae but are difficult in the case of cyanidialean red algae because their last common acidophilic ancestor diverged from other neutrophilic red algae 1.2–1.3 billion y ago (<a id="xref-ref-12-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-12">12</a>). Thus, whole-genome comparisons between evolutionarily related neutrophilic and acidophilic green algae will give insights into how acidophiles evolved from their neutrophilic ancestors.</p><p id="p-9">Here, we determined the draft genome of the acidophilic green alga <em>Chlamydomonas eustigma</em> NIES-2499 isolated from sulfuric AMD and performed comparative genome and transcriptome analyses between <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em> and its neutrophilic relative <em>Chlamydomonas reinhardtii</em>, which was previously fully sequenced (<a id="xref-ref-13-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-13">13</a>). The results suggest that up-regulation of genes encoding heat-shock proteins (HSPs) and plasma membrane H<sup>+</sup>-ATPase (PMA), loss of fermentative genes that produce organic acids and thus reduce cytosolic pH, the acquisition of an energy shuttle and buffering system, and the acquisition and multiplication of genes involved in arsenic biotransformation and detoxification have contributed to the adaptation of <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em> to acidic conditions. The results also suggest that there are several commonalities in genomic evolution for adapting to acidic environments among red algae and green algae.</p><div class="section results" id="sec-1"><h2 class="">Results</h2><div id="sec-2" class="subsection"><h3>Habitat, Taxonomic Position, and Physiological Features of the Acidophilic Green Alga <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em>.</h3><p id="p-10"><em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em> (a haploid vegetative cell) was originally isolated together with mosses (<a id="xref-ref-14-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-14">14</a>) from sulfuric AMD in Nagano Prefecture, Japan, in August 1992. We confirmed that <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em> still thrived in that AMD (pH 2.13, 14.5 °C) in September 2013 (<a id="xref-fig-1-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F1">Fig. 1<em>A</em></a>). The water in the AMD contained high concentrations of iron, Al<sup>3+</sup>, and SO<sub>4</sub><sup>2−</sup> (<a id="xref-fig-1-3" class="xref-fig" href="#F1">Fig. 1<em>B</em></a>), as in the case of other AMDs (<a id="xref-ref-15-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-15">15</a>). <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em> exhibits a cell size and morphology very similar to those of the neutrophilic <em>C</em>. <em>reinhardtii</em>. Both cells possess two flagella and a large cup-shaped chloroplast in which an eyespot and a large pyrenoid are formed and proliferate by forming autospores in the mother cell (<a id="xref-fig-1-4" class="xref-fig" href="#F1">Fig. 1<em>C</em></a>). As also shown by a previous phylogenetic study based on 18S rDNA sequences (<a id="xref-ref-16-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-16">16</a>), phylogenetic analysis based on five chloroplast-encoded genes and two chloroplast ribosomal DNA sequences (<a id="xref-table-wrap-1-1" class="xref-table" href="#T1">Table S1</a>) showed that <em>C. eustigma</em>, together with the fully sequenced neutrophile <em>C. reinhardtii</em> (<a id="xref-ref-13-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-13">13</a>), belongs to the Chlorophyceae, which contains mainly neutrophilic algae (<a id="xref-fig-1-5" class="xref-fig" href="#F1">Fig. 1<em>D</em></a>). In the phylogenetic tree, all the Chlorophyta (green algae) except for <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em> are neutrophilic (<a id="xref-fig-1-6" class="xref-fig" href="#F1">Fig. 1<em>D</em></a>), suggesting that the acidophile <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em> evolved from a neutrophilic ancestor. In autotrophic synthetic medium at 20 °C, <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em> proliferated at pH 2.0–6.0 (at pH 1.0 cells grew for a few days, but after that they died), and pH 3.0–6.0 was optimal for its growth, whereas <em>C</em>. <em>reinhardtii</em> proliferated at pH 5.0–8.0, and pH 6.0–7.0 was optimal for its growth (<a id="xref-fig-1-7" class="xref-fig" href="#F1">Fig. 1 <em>E</em> and <em>F</em></a>).</p><div id="T1" class="table pos-float type-supplementary-material"><div class="table-inline table-callout-links"><div class="callout"><span>View this table:</span><ul class="callout-links"><li class="view-inline first"><a href="##" class="table-expand-inline" data-table-url="/highwire/markup/27930/expansion?postprocessors=highwire_tables%2Chighwire_reclass%2Chighwire_figures%2Chighwire_math%2Chighwire_inline_linked_media%2Chighwire_embed&amp;table-expand-inline=1" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">View inline</a></li>
<li class="view-popup last"><a href="/highwire/markup/27930/expansion?width=1000&amp;height=500&amp;iframe=true&amp;postprocessors=highwire_tables%2Chighwire_reclass%2Chighwire_figures%2Chighwire_math%2Chighwire_inline_linked_media%2Chighwire_embed" class="colorbox colorbox-load table-expand-popup" rel="gallery-fragment-tables" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">View popup</a></li>
</ul></div></div><div class="table-caption"><span class="table-label">Table S1.</span> <p id="p-11" class="first-child">Taxon sampling for the phylogeny based on five chloroplast-encoded genes and two chloroplast rDNA sequences</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div></div><div id="sec-3" class="subsection"><h3>Characteristics of the <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em> Nuclear Genome.</h3><p id="p-13">To understand the genetic basis of the adaptation of <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em> to an acidic environment, we sequenced its nuclear genome (<a id="xref-table-wrap-2-1" class="xref-table" href="#T2">Tables S2</a> and <a id="xref-table-wrap-3-1" class="xref-table" href="#T3">S3</a>). K-mer analysis of Illumina MiSeq reads yielded two peaks with similar frequency of coverage (19× and 38×, respectively), suggesting that the <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em> genome is a chimera of single and duplicated regions (<a id="xref-fig-2-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F2">Fig. 2<em>A</em></a>). Considering the length of the duplicated regions, the estimated genome size was ∼130 Mb (<a id="xref-table-wrap-3-2" class="xref-table" href="#T3">Table S3</a>). Then we obtained Illumina HiSeq and Roche 454 GS FLX+ reads of the nuclear genome (<a id="xref-table-wrap-2-2" class="xref-table" href="#T2">Table S2</a>). The sequenced DNA reads were assembled into 519 scaffolds (the N<sub>50</sub> scaffold size was 465 kb, and the total length was 67 Mb) (<a id="xref-table-wrap-3-3" class="xref-table" href="#T3">Table S3</a>). Consistent with the result of K-mer analysis, sequencing coverage ratios of some scaffolds were two times those of other scaffolds (<a id="xref-fig-2-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F2">Fig. 2<em>B</em></a> and <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1707072114/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1707072114.sd01.xlsx" class="in-nw">Dataset S1</a>).</p><div id="F2" class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/114/39/E8304/F2.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="The C. eustigma genome architecture and comparison of genome contents between C. eustigma and C. reinhardtii. (A) 31 K-mer depth distribution of whole-genome Illumina MiSeq reads. Two peaks at 19&#xD7; and 38&#xD7; were identified. </ul></div></div><div class="table-caption"><span class="table-label">Table S2.</span> <p id="p-15" class="first-child">Summary of the Roche 454 and Illumina HiSeq and MiSeq data used for this study</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div><div id="T3" class="table pos-float type-supplementary-material"><div class="table-inline table-callout-links"><div class="callout"><span>View this table:</span><ul class="callout-links"><li class="view-inline first"><a href="##" class="table-expand-inline" data-table-url="/highwire/markup/27936/expansion?postprocessors=highwire_tables%2Chighwire_reclass%2Chighwire_figures%2Chighwire_math%2Chighwire_inline_linked_media%2Chighwire_embed&amp;table-expand-inline=1" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">View inline</a></li>
<li class="view-popup last"><a href="/highwire/markup/27936/expansion?width=1000&amp;height=500&amp;iframe=true&amp;postprocessors=highwire_tables%2Chighwire_reclass%2Chighwire_figures%2Chighwire_math%2Chighwire_inline_linked_media%2Chighwire_embed" class="colorbox colorbox-load table-expand-popup" rel="gallery-fragment-tables" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">View popup</a></li>
</ul></div></div><div class="table-caption"><span class="table-label">Table S3.</span> <p id="p-16" class="first-child">Summary of statistics for genome-level analyses of <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em> and <em>C</em>. <em>reinhardtii</em></p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div><p id="p-18">Most of the scaffolds consist of only single or duplicated sequences, with a few exceptions (for example, the largest scaffold is a chimera of single and duplicated regions) (<a id="xref-fig-2-3" class="xref-fig" href="#F2">Fig. 2<em>B</em></a> and <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1707072114/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1707072114.sd01.xlsx" class="in-nw">Dataset S1</a>). This result suggests that <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em> has experienced genomic duplication at the chromosomal level but not considerable rearrangements of the duplicated regions. Considering the length of the duplicated regions, the assembled genome size of <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em> was ∼110 Mb (<a id="xref-table-wrap-3-4" class="xref-table" href="#T3">Table S3</a>). The difference between the estimated (∼130 Mb) and assembled (∼110 Mb) genome sizes is probably due to the difficulty in resolving repeats, which is often encountered in genome sequencing studies (<a id="xref-ref-17-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-17">17</a>). The <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em> genome exhibits relatively low GC content (45%) compared with the genomes of other green algae (64% in <em>C</em>. <em>reinhardtii</em>; 56% in <em>Volvox carteri</em>; 67% in <em>Chlorella variabilis</em>; 53% in <em>Coccomyxa subellipsoidea</em>) (<a id="xref-fig-2-4" class="xref-fig" href="#F2">Fig. 2<em>C</em></a>).</p><p id="p-19">In the assembled <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em> draft genome, 14,105 protein-coding genes were identified by Augustus software with RNA-sequencing (RNA-seq) data (genes encoded in duplicated regions are counted as single genes) (<a id="xref-table-wrap-3-5" class="xref-table" href="#T3">Table S3</a>). The BLASTP search against the Nationl Center for Biotechnology Information nonredundant (NCBI-nr) database (release 20160519) showed that 52.1% of <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em> proteins are most closely related to those of Volvocales (<em>C</em>. <em>reinhardtii</em>, <em>Gonium pectorale</em>, and <em>V</em>. <em>carteri</em>), whereas 19.7% showed no significant similarity to any known proteins (<a id="xref-fig-3-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F3">Fig. S1</a>).</p><div id="F3" class="fig pos-float type-supplementary-material  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/114/39/E8304/F3.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="BLAST top-hit analysis of C. eustigma proteins against the NCBI-nr database. The pie chart shows the species distribution of the top BLASTP hits of the C. eustigma proteins (total 14,105 sequences). The BLASTP search was conducted with an e-value cutoff of 1e&#x2212;5." class="highwire-fragment fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-1059355296" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;div xmlns=&quot;http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml&quot;&gt;BLAST top-hit analysis of &lt;em&gt;C&lt;/em&gt;. &lt;em&gt;eustigma&lt;/em&gt; proteins against the NCBI-nr database. The pie chart shows the species distribution of the top BLASTP hits of the &lt;em&gt;C&lt;/em&gt;. &lt;em&gt;eustigma&lt;/em&gt; proteins (total 14,105 sequences). The BLASTP search was conducted with an e-value cutoff of 1e&lt;sup&gt;&#x2212;5&lt;/sup&gt;.&lt;/div&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image lazyload" alt="Fig. S1." src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/114/39/E8304/F3.medium.gif" width="440" height="281"/><noscript><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image" alt="Fig. S1." src="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/114/39/E8304/F3.medium.gif" width="440" height="281"/></noscript></span></a></div></div><ul class="highwire-figure-links inline"><li class="download-fig first"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/114/39/E8304/F3.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download" title="Download Fig. S1." data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download figure</a></li>
<li class="new-tab"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/114/39/E8304/F3.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab" target="_blank" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Open in new tab</a></li>
<li class="download-ppt last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/27937" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download powerpoint</a></li>
</ul></div><div class="fig-caption"><span class="fig-label">Fig. S1.</span> <p id="p-20" class="first-child">BLAST top-hit analysis of <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em> proteins against the NCBI-nr database. The pie chart shows the species distribution of the top BLASTP hits of the <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em> proteins (total 14,105 sequences). The BLASTP search was conducted with an e-value cutoff of 1e<sup>−5</sup>.</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div></div><div id="sec-4" class="subsection"><h3>High Expression of <em>HSP</em> and <em>PMA</em> Genes in <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em>.</h3><p id="p-21">To compare the genomic contents between acidophile <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em> and neutrophile <em>C</em>. <em>reinhardtii</em>, functional annotations were assigned to <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em> and <em>C</em>. <em>reinhardtii</em> gene models. Predicted genes were assigned to the Kyoto Encyclopedia of Genes and Genomes (KEGG) Orthology database through the KEGG Automatic Annotation Server (KAAS). The analysis assigned unique KEGG Orthology IDs to 4,470 <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em> and 4,741 <em>C</em>. <em>reinhardtii</em> protein-coding genes, respectively (<a id="xref-table-wrap-3-6" class="xref-table" href="#T3">Table S3</a>). However, there were no marked differences in the number of genes classified into respective functional categories (<a id="xref-fig-2-5" class="xref-fig" href="#F2">Fig. 2<em>D</em></a>), and most of the KEGG Orthology IDs (3,006) were shared by the two species (<a id="xref-fig-2-6" class="xref-fig" href="#F2">Fig. 2<em>E</em></a>).</p><p id="p-22">To examine the difference in the expression levels of the orthologous genes between <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em> and <em>C</em>. <em>reinhardtii</em>, we performed RNA-seq analyses of the two species under their individual optimal conditions (at 20 °C in the same autotrophic medium and at pH 3.0 for <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em> and pH 7.0 for <em>C</em>. <em>reinhardtii</em>). Before comparing the transcriptome, we identified 4,590 one-to-one orthologous genes in the three volvocalean species (<em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em>, <em>C</em>. <em>reinhardtii</em>, and <em>V</em>. <em>carteri</em>) by OrthoMCL (<a id="xref-fig-4-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F4">Fig. 3 <em>A</em> and <em>B</em></a>). Of the 4,590 genes, 1,282 (∼30%) showed a greater than fivefold difference in the mRNA levels between <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em> and <em>C</em>. <em>reinhardtii</em> (<a id="xref-fig-4-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F4">Fig. 3<em>C</em></a> and <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1707072114/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1707072114.sd02.xlsx" class="in-nw">Dataset S2</a>). Notably, in the group that was up-regulated in <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em>, <em>HSP</em> genes were enriched (<a id="xref-fig-4-3" class="xref-fig" href="#F4">Fig. 3 <em>C</em> and <em>D</em></a> and <a id="xref-fig-5-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F5">Fig. S2</a>). Consistent with the result at the mRNA level, previous studies showed that the acidophile <em>C</em>. <em>acidophila</em> (CCAP 11/137 isolated from acidic fresh water in Germany), which is closely related to <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em> (<a id="xref-ref-16-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-16">16</a>), had higher basal HSP levels (HSP70, HSP60, and HSP20) than <em>C</em>. <em>reinhardtii</em> (<a id="xref-ref-18-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-18">18</a>). These observations suggest that <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em> is constantly exposed to higher stress despite being adapted to an acidic environment.</p><div id="F4" class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/114/39/E8304/F4.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Comparison of genome contents and transcriptome between the acidophile C. eustigma and neutrophile C. reinhardtii. (A) Venn diagram showing the number of protein families (by OrthoMCL) shared by C. eustigma, C. reinhardtii, and V. carteri genomes. (B) Gene orthologs of C. eustigma, C. reinhardtii, and V. carteri identified by OrthoMCL. &#x201C;1:1:1&#x201D; indicates an ortholog shared by three species as single copies; &#x201C;N:N:N&#x201D; indicates an ortholog shared by three species as multiple copies; &#x201C;Patchy&#x201D; indicates an ortholog shared by only two species. &#x201C;ND&#x201D; and &#x201C;SD&#x201D; indicate a species-specific gene in single or multiple copies, respectively. </ul></div><div class="fig-caption"><span class="fig-label">Fig. 3.</span> <p id="p-23" class="first-child">Comparison of genome contents and transcriptome between the acidophile <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em> and neutrophile <em>C</em>. <em>reinhardtii</em>. (<em>A</em>) Venn diagram showing the number of protein families (by OrthoMCL) shared by <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em>, <em>C</em>. <em>reinhardtii</em>, and <em>V</em>. <em>carteri</em> genomes. The RPKM levels of 4,590 orthologous genes were plotted. (<em>D</em>) Comparison of RPKM levels of the <em>PMA</em> gene and <em>HSP</em> genes in <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em> (pH 3.0, 20 °C) and <em>C</em>. <em>reinhardtii</em> (pH 7.0, 20 °C). The number above the bar indicates the number of genes that exist in the respective nuclear genomes.</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div><div id="F5" class="fig pos-float type-supplementary-material  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/114/39/E8304/F5.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="The relative levels of mRNAs in C. eustigma (pH 3.0, 20 &#xB0;C), C. reinhardtii (pH 7.0, 20 &#xB0;C), and the red alga Cy. merolae (pH 2.2, 42 &#xB0;C). The left histograms show a magnified view of the right histograms. AMGT, amidinotransferase; GR, glutathione reductase; Grx, glutaredoxin; HSP, heat-shock protein; PK, phosphagen kinase; PMA, plasma membrane H+-ATPase." class="highwire-fragment fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-1059355296" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;div xmlns=&quot;http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml&quot;&gt;The relative levels of mRNAs in &lt;em&gt;C&lt;/em&gt;. &lt;em&gt;eustigma&lt;/em&gt; (pH 3.0, 20 &#xB0;C), &lt;em&gt;C&lt;/em&gt;. &lt;em&gt;reinhardtii&lt;/em&gt; (pH 7.0, 20 &#xB0;C), and the red alga &lt;em&gt;Cy&lt;/em&gt;. &lt;em&gt;merolae&lt;/em&gt; (pH 2.2, 42 &#xB0;C). The left histograms show a magnified view of the right histograms. AMGT, amidinotransferase; GR, glutathione reductase; Grx, glutaredoxin; HSP, heat-shock protein; PK, phosphagen kinase; PMA, plasma membrane H&lt;sup&gt;+&lt;/sup&gt;-ATPase.&lt;/div&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image lazyload" alt="Fig. S2." src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/114/39/E8304/F5.medium.gif" width="440" height="227"/><noscript><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image" alt="Fig. S2." src="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/114/39/E8304/F5.medium.gif" width="440" height="227"/></noscript></span></a></div></div><ul class="highwire-figure-links inline"><li class="download-fig first"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/114/39/E8304/F5.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download" title="Download Fig. S2." data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download figure</a></li>
<li class="new-tab"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/114/39/E8304/F5.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab" target="_blank" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Open in new tab</a></li>
<li class="download-ppt last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/27941" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download powerpoint</a></li>
</ul></div><div class="fig-caption"><span class="fig-label">Fig. S2.</span> <p id="p-24" class="first-child">The relative levels of mRNAs in <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em> (pH 3.0, 20 °C), <em>C</em>. <em>reinhardtii</em> (pH 7.0, 20 °C), and the red alga <em>Cy</em>. <em>merolae</em> (pH 2.2, 42 °C). The left histograms show a magnified view of the right histograms. AMGT, amidinotransferase; GR, glutathione reductase; Grx, glutaredoxin; HSP, heat-shock protein; PK, phosphagen kinase; PMA, plasma membrane H<sup>+</sup>-ATPase.</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div><p id="p-25">In addition, we found that <em>PMA</em> was highly expressed in <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em> [151th highest reads per kilobase of transcript per million reads mapped (RPKM) value among 14,105 protein-coding genes] compared with <em>C</em>. <em>reinhardtii</em> (1,553th highest RPKM value among 17,741 protein-coding genes) (<a id="xref-fig-4-4" class="xref-fig" href="#F4">Fig. 3 <em>C</em> and <em>D</em></a> and <a id="xref-fig-5-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F5">Fig. S2</a>). Maintenance of a neutral pH in the cytosol despite being in an acidic environment of pH 3 indicates the presence of a 10<sup>4</sup>-fold proton gradient across the plasma membrane. It has been suggested that this proton gradient in acidophiles is achieved by a combination of active transport and low permeability of protons (<a id="xref-ref-19-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-19">19</a>). In the acidophile <em>Chlamydomonas</em> sp. (ATCC PRA-125 isolated from acidic fresh water in Spain), it was previously shown that average cytosolic pH is maintained at pH 6.6 in the culture medium at both pH 2 and pH 7 (<a id="xref-ref-20-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-20">20</a>). In <em>C</em>. <em>reinhardtii</em> cultured at pH 7, the average cytosolic pH was 7.1 (<a id="xref-ref-20-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-20">20</a>). In addition, it was shown that 7% more ATP was consumed to remove protons entering the cytosol across the membrane at pH 2 than at pH 7 (<a id="xref-ref-20-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-20">20</a>). Thus, the high expression of <em>PMA</em> in <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em> probably contributes to maintaining the high proton-pumping activity against the acidic environment.</p></div><div id="sec-5" class="subsection"><h3>Selective Loss of Acid-Producing Fermentation Pathways from <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em>.</h3><p id="p-26">The above comparison of genome contents showed that several hundred KEGG Orthology IDs (<a id="xref-fig-2-7" class="xref-fig" href="#F2">Fig. 2<em>E</em></a>) and gene families (<a id="xref-fig-4-5" class="xref-fig" href="#F4">Fig. 3<em>A</em></a>) are specific to either <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em> or <em>C</em>. <em>reinhardtii</em>, suggesting that the gene acquisitions and gene losses by <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em> after divergence from its neutrophilic ancestor also played roles in its adaptation to an acidic environment.</p><p id="p-27">Regarding the gene losses by <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em>, we found that the genome had lost many genes involved in anaerobic fermentation pathways (<a id="xref-fig-6-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F6">Fig. 4 <em>A</em> and <em>B</em></a>). Several lineages of eukaryotic algae have evolved fermentation pathways that produce ATP when oxygenic respiration is compromised, for example, under anoxic/hypoxic conditions resulting from a low level of photosynthetic activity and local depletion of oxygen by microbial respiration (<a id="xref-ref-21-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-21">21</a>). The alcohol fermentation pathway produces a diffusible, nonacidic, and relatively nontoxic end product, ethanol, while other pathways produce organic acids as end products that cause cytosolic acidification and damage (<a id="xref-fig-6-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F6">Fig. 4<em>A</em></a>) (<a id="xref-ref-22-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-22">22</a><a id="xref-ref-23-1" class="xref-down-link" href="#ref-23"><span>⇓</span></a>–<a id="xref-ref-24-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-24">24</a>). Moreover, organic acids function as uncouplers of the respiratory chain at low pH by diffusion of the protonated form into the cell, followed by dissociation of a proton (<a id="xref-ref-19-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-19">19</a>).</p><div id="F6" class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/114/39/E8304/F6.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Loss of organic acid-producing fermentation pathways by C. eustigma. (A) Overview of the anaerobic fermentation pathways in eukaryotic algae (24, 66). Glucose (stored as starch) synthesized by photosynthesis is oxidized to pyruvate via glycolysis. Under anaerobic conditions, the conversion of pyruvate to acetyl-coA is catalyzed by the pyruvate formate lyase (PFL1) pathway that generates formate or by the pyruvate-ferredoxin oxidoreductase (PFR) and hydrogenase (HYDA) pathway that generates hydrogen. In C. reinhardtii, HYDEF and HYDG are essential to activate HYDA (67). Acetyl-CoA enters the phosphate acetyltransferase (PAT) and acetate kinase (ACK) pathway that generates acetate or the aldehyde/alcohol dehydrogenase (ADHE) pathway that generates ethanol. Pyruvate can also be used as a substrate to generate ethanol or lactate via the PDC3 (pyruvate decarboxylase 3) and alcohol dehydrogenase (ADH) pathway or lactate dehydrogenase (LDH) pathway, respectively. Acetate is used for lipid biosynthesis or is converted into acetyl-CoA by acetyl-CoA synthetase (ACS), which is further processed in the glyoxylate cycle to regenerate malate and succinate. ACO, aconitase; CIT, citrate synthase; ICL, isocitrate lyase; MDH, malate dehydrogenase; MLS, malate synthase; PEP, phosphoenolpyruvate. (B) Presence or absence of fermentation genes in the genomes of five green algae (shown in green) and two thermo-acidophilic red algae (shown in red). The red boxes indicate the presence of the gene, and white boxes indicate the absence of the gene. (C) Concentrations of lactate, formate, acetate, and ethanol in the algal culture medium before (0 h) and 4 h after the dark anaerobic treatment. The error bars represent the SD of three biological replicates. DW, dry weight; ND, not detected; NS, not statistically significant; *P &lt; 0.02, **P &lt; 0.01 (t test)." class="highwire-fragment fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-1059355296" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;div xmlns=&quot;http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml&quot;&gt;Loss of organic acid-producing fermentation pathways by &lt;em&gt;C&lt;/em&gt;. &lt;em&gt;eustigma&lt;/em&gt;. (&lt;em&gt;A&lt;/em&gt;) Overview of the anaerobic fermentation pathways in eukaryotic algae (24, 66). Glucose (stored as starch) synthesized by photosynthesis is oxidized to pyruvate via glycolysis. Under anaerobic conditions, the conversion of pyruvate to acetyl-coA is catalyzed by the pyruvate formate lyase (PFL1) pathway that generates formate or by the pyruvate-ferredoxin oxidoreductase (PFR) and hydrogenase (HYDA) pathway that generates hydrogen. In &lt;em&gt;C&lt;/em&gt;. &lt;em&gt;reinhardtii&lt;/em&gt;, HYDEF and HYDG are essential to activate HYDA (67). Acetyl-CoA enters the phosphate acetyltransferase (PAT) and acetate kinase (ACK) pathway that generates acetate or the aldehyde/alcohol dehydrogenase (ADHE) pathway that generates ethanol. Pyruvate can also be used as a substrate to generate ethanol or lactate via the PDC3 (pyruvate decarboxylase 3) and alcohol dehydrogenase (ADH) pathway or lactate dehydrogenase (LDH) pathway, respectively. Acetate is used for lipid biosynthesis or is converted into acetyl-CoA by acetyl-CoA synthetase (ACS), which is further processed in the glyoxylate cycle to regenerate malate and succinate. ACO, aconitase; CIT, citrate synthase; ICL, isocitrate lyase; MDH, malate dehydrogenase; MLS, malate synthase; PEP, phosphoenolpyruvate. (&lt;em&gt;B&lt;/em&gt;) Presence or absence of fermentation genes in the genomes of five green algae (shown in green) and two thermo-acidophilic red algae (shown in red). The red boxes indicate the presence of the gene, and white boxes indicate the absence of the gene. (&lt;em&gt;C&lt;/em&gt;) Concentrations of lactate, formate, acetate, and ethanol in the algal culture medium before (0 h) and 4 h after the dark anaerobic treatment. The error bars represent the SD of three biological replicates. DW, dry weight; ND, not detected; NS, not statistically significant; *&lt;em&gt;P&lt;/em&gt; &lt; 0.02, **&lt;em&gt;P&lt;/em&gt; &lt; 0.01 (&lt;em&gt;t&lt;/em&gt; test).&lt;/div&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image lazyload" alt="Fig. 4." src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/114/39/E8304/F6.medium.gif" width="378" height="440"/><noscript><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image" alt="Fig. 4." src="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/114/39/E8304/F6.medium.gif" width="378" height="440"/></noscript></span></a></div></div><ul class="highwire-figure-links inline"><li class="download-fig first"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/114/39/E8304/F6.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download" title="Download Fig. 4." data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download figure</a></li>
<li class="new-tab"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/114/39/E8304/F6.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab" target="_blank" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Open in new tab</a></li>
<li class="download-ppt last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/27943" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download powerpoint</a></li>
</ul></div><div class="fig-caption"><span class="fig-label">Fig. 4.</span> <p id="p-28" class="first-child">Loss of organic acid-producing fermentation pathways by <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em>. (<em>A</em>) Overview of the anaerobic fermentation pathways in eukaryotic algae (<a id="xref-ref-24-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-24">24</a>, <a id="xref-ref-66-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-66">66</a>). Glucose (stored as starch) synthesized by photosynthesis is oxidized to pyruvate via glycolysis. Under anaerobic conditions, the conversion of pyruvate to acetyl-coA is catalyzed by the pyruvate formate lyase (PFL1) pathway that generates formate or by the pyruvate-ferredoxin oxidoreductase (PFR) and hydrogenase (HYDA) pathway that generates hydrogen. In <em>C</em>. <em>reinhardtii</em>, HYDEF and HYDG are essential to activate HYDA (<a id="xref-ref-67-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-67">67</a>). Acetyl-CoA enters the phosphate acetyltransferase (PAT) and acetate kinase (ACK) pathway that generates acetate or the aldehyde/alcohol dehydrogenase (ADHE) pathway that generates ethanol. Pyruvate can also be used as a substrate to generate ethanol or lactate via the PDC3 (pyruvate decarboxylase 3) and alcohol dehydrogenase (ADH) pathway or lactate dehydrogenase (LDH) pathway, respectively. Acetate is used for lipid biosynthesis or is converted into acetyl-CoA by acetyl-CoA synthetase (ACS), which is further processed in the glyoxylate cycle to regenerate malate and succinate. ACO, aconitase; CIT, citrate synthase; ICL, isocitrate lyase; MDH, malate dehydrogenase; MLS, malate synthase; PEP, phosphoenolpyruvate. (<em>B</em>) Presence or absence of fermentation genes in the genomes of five green algae (shown in green) and two thermo-acidophilic red algae (shown in red). The red boxes indicate the presence of the gene, and white boxes indicate the absence of the gene. (<em>C</em>) Concentrations of lactate, formate, acetate, and ethanol in the algal culture medium before (0 h) and 4 h after the dark anaerobic treatment. The error bars represent the SD of three biological replicates. DW, dry weight; ND, not detected; NS, not statistically significant; *<em>P</em> &lt; 0.02, **<em>P</em> &lt; 0.01 (<em>t</em> test).</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div><p id="p-29"><em>C</em>. <em>reinhardtii</em> possesses fermentation pathways that produce both ethanol and organic acids, namely, lactate, formate, and acetate (<a id="xref-fig-6-3" class="xref-fig" href="#F6">Fig. 4 <em>A</em> and <em>B</em></a>) (<a id="xref-ref-24-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-24">24</a>). Although the <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em> genome encodes pyruvate decarboxylase 3 (PDC3) and alcohol dehydrogenase (ADH) that produce ethanol, it lacks enzymes involved in organic acid fermentation pathways, such as lactate dehydrogenase (LDH) that produces lactate, pyruvate formate lyase (PFL) that produces formate, and both chloroplast and mitochondrial phosphate acetyltransferases (PAT2 and PAT1) and acetate kinases (ACK1 and ACK2) that produce acetate (<a id="xref-fig-6-4" class="xref-fig" href="#F6">Fig. 4 <em>A</em> and <em>B</em></a>). In addition to lacking these genes, <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em> lacks the genes encoding pyruvate:ferredoxin oxidoreductase (PFR) and hydrogenase (HYDA) and the proteins required for hydrogenase activation (HYDEF and HYDG) (<a id="xref-ref-25-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-25">25</a>). All the above-mentioned genes absent in <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em> are present in other green algal genomes (<em>C</em>. <em>reinhardtii</em>, <em>V</em>. <em>carteri</em>, and <em>Ch</em>. <em>Variabilis</em>) (<a id="xref-fig-6-5" class="xref-fig" href="#F6">Fig. 4<em>B</em></a> and <a id="xref-table-wrap-4-1" class="xref-table" href="#T4">Table S4</a>), suggesting that <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em> lost these genes during evolution after divergence from the common ancestor of <em>C</em>. <em>reinhardtii</em> and <em>V</em>. <em>carteri</em>. We found that some enzymes are also absent in <em>Co</em>. <em>subellipsoidea</em> C-169 (isolated from Antarctic dried algal peat) (<a id="xref-ref-26-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-26">26</a>). However, based on the phylogenetic relationship between <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em> and <em>Co</em>. <em>subellipsoidea</em>, they probably lost these genes independently (<a id="xref-fig-6-6" class="xref-fig" href="#F6">Fig. 4<em>B</em></a>).</p><div id="T4" class="table pos-float type-supplementary-material"><div class="table-inline table-callout-links"><div class="callout"><span>View this table:</span><ul class="callout-links"><li class="view-inline first"><a href="##" class="table-expand-inline" data-table-url="/highwire/markup/27946/expansion?postprocessors=highwire_tables%2Chighwire_reclass%2Chighwire_figures%2Chighwire_math%2Chighwire_inline_linked_media%2Chighwire_embed&amp;table-expand-inline=1" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">View inline</a></li>
<li class="view-popup last"><a href="/highwire/markup/27946/expansion?width=1000&amp;height=500&amp;iframe=true&amp;postprocessors=highwire_tables%2Chighwire_reclass%2Chighwire_figures%2Chighwire_math%2Chighwire_inline_linked_media%2Chighwire_embed" class="colorbox colorbox-load table-expand-popup" rel="gallery-fragment-tables" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">View popup</a></li>
</ul></div></div><div class="table-caption"><span class="table-label">Table S4.</span> <p id="p-30" class="first-child">Genes involved in anaerobic energy metabolism in <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em>, <em>C</em>. <em>reinhardtii</em>, <em>V</em>. <em>carteri</em>, <em>Co</em>. <em>subellipsoidea</em>, <em>Ch</em>. <em>variabilis</em>, <em>Cy</em>. <em>merolae</em>, and <em>G</em>. <em>sulphuraria</em></p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div><p id="p-32">Consistent with the loss of genes involved in organic acid fermentation, HPLC analyses showed that <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em> produces little lactate, formate, and acetate (<a id="xref-fig-6-7" class="xref-fig" href="#F6">Fig. 4<em>C</em></a>). These three organic acids were detected in the supernatant fraction of <em>C</em>. <em>reinhardtii</em> culture but were scarcely detected in that of <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em> under aerobic conditions (<a id="xref-fig-6-8" class="xref-fig" href="#F6">Fig. 4<em>C</em></a>). When the cells were transferred to dark and anaerobic conditions, the formate and acetate levels increased in the supernatant fraction of <em>C. reinhardtii</em> culture 4 h after the transfer (<a id="xref-fig-6-9" class="xref-fig" href="#F6">Fig. 4<em>C</em></a>), as previously reported (<a id="xref-ref-27-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-27">27</a>), but under these conditions these organic acids still were hardly detected in the supernatant fraction of <em>C. eustigma</em> (<a id="xref-fig-6-10" class="xref-fig" href="#F6">Fig. 4<em>C</em></a>). In contrast to the loss of organic acid fermentation by <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em>, a higher concentration of ethanol was detected in the supernatant fraction of <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em> culture than in that of <em>C</em>. <em>reinhardtii</em> by the gas chromatography analysis (<a id="xref-fig-6-11" class="xref-fig" href="#F6">Fig. 4<em>C</em></a>). The cellular ethanol level increased 4 h after the cells had been transferred from aerobic to dark and anaerobic conditions in both <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em> and <em>C</em>. <em>reinhardtii</em> (<a id="xref-fig-6-12" class="xref-fig" href="#F6">Fig. 4<em>C</em></a>). These results indicate that <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em> selectively lost organic acid-producing fermentative genes.</p><p id="p-33">In the rice bean <em>Vigna umbellata</em>, it was reported that exposure of plants to low pH leads to the accumulation of formate. In addition, overexpression of <em>V</em>. <em>umbellata</em> formate dehydrogenase in tobacco resulted in decreased sensitivity to low pH and aluminum stresses by reducing the accumulation of formate (<a id="xref-ref-28-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-28">28</a>). Thus, the loss of organic acid-producing fermentation pathways by <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em> probably contributed to the adaptation to acidic environments with high concentrations of metals. This probably also accounts for the independent loss of fermentation genes from <em>Co</em>. <em>subellipsoidea</em> because the genus <em>Coccomyxa</em> contains several acidophilic members (<a id="xref-ref-29-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-29">29</a>, <a id="xref-ref-30-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-30">30</a>).</p><p id="p-34">The genome analyses also showed that <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em> had lost the key enzymes of the glyoxylate cycle, namely, malate synthase (MLS) and isocitrate lyase (ICL) (<a id="xref-fig-6-13" class="xref-fig" href="#F6">Fig. 4 <em>A</em> and <em>B</em></a>). The glyoxylate cycle converts acetyl-CoA to succinate for the synthesis of carbohydrates and plays an essential role in cell growth on acetate (<a id="xref-ref-31-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-31">31</a>). The loss of these enzymes is probably consistent with the fact that <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em> produces little acetate (<a id="xref-fig-6-14" class="xref-fig" href="#F6">Fig. 4<em>C</em></a>) and/or prevents cytosolic acidification that is caused by succinate production.</p></div><div id="sec-6" class="subsection"><h3>Acquisition of the Energy Shuttle and Buffering System Based on Amidinotransferase and Phosphagen Kinase by <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em> Through HGT.</h3><p id="p-35">Regarding the gene acquisition by <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em>, we found that the genome encodes two phosphagen kinases (PKs) and one amidinotransferase (AMGT), which were probably introduced through HGT (<a id="xref-fig-7-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F7">Fig. 5 <em>A</em>–<em>C</em></a> and <a id="xref-fig-8-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F8">Fig. S3</a>). PK and AMGT exist in various animal, protozoan, and bacterial taxa and function as an energy shuttle and buffering system (<a id="xref-ref-32-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-32">32</a>) (<a id="xref-fig-7-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F7">Fig. 5<em>C</em></a>). PK catalyzes the reversible transfer of a phosphate between ATP and guanidino compounds (e.g., arginine, creatine, glycocyamine, lombricine, and taurocyamine), which are produced from amino acids by AMGT (<a id="xref-fig-7-3" class="xref-fig" href="#F7">Fig. 5<em>C</em></a>) (<a id="xref-ref-32-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-32">32</a>). However, <em>PK</em> or <em>AMGT</em> genes have not been identified in other Archaeplastida (land plants and eukaryotic algae whose chloroplasts are of cyanobacterial primary endosymbiotic origin) (<a id="xref-ref-11-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-11">11</a>).</p><div id="F7" class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/114/39/E8304/F7.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Acquisition of the PK&#x2013;AMGT energy shuttle and buffering system by C. eustigma through HGT. (A) Genomic location of the two PK genes and one AMGT gene. PK1 and AMGT are encoded in the scaffold Ceu0008, and PK2 is encoded in the scaffold Ceu0033 in the C. eustigma genome. (B) Phylogenetic relationship of PK proteins. The tree was constructed by the ML method (RaxML 8.0.0). ML BP &gt;50% obtained by RaxML, and BI &gt;0.95 obtained by Bayesian analysis (MrBayes 3.2.6) are shown above the branches. The accession numbers of the sequences are shown along with the names of the species. The branch lengths reflect the evolutionary distances indicated by the scale bar. (C) Overview of the phosphagen kinase energy buffering system (32). Taurocyamine (Tac) is produced by amidinotransferase (AMGT) from l-arginine (Arg) and taurine (Tau). Tac is phosphorylated to produce phosphotaurocyamine (PTac) by mitochondrial taurocyamine kinase (mtTK) by consuming ATP produced by oxidative phosphorylation. PTac is dephosphorylated by cytosolic taurocyamine kinase (cytTK) to produce ATP. ATP is, for example, consumed by plasma membrane H+-ATPase (PMA) to pump protons from the cytosol to outside the cell. Orn, ornithine. (D) Amino acid sequence alignment of the guanidine specificity region in C. eustigma phosphagen kinase with those of other organisms. The guanidine specificity regions (33) are shaded red, and taurocyamine kinases are shaded green. D1 and D2 represent domain 1 and domain 2, respectively, of two-domain enzymes. AvGK, Alitta virens glycocyamine kinase; CePK, C. eustigma phosphagen kinase; EfLK, Eisenia fetida lombricine kinase; LpAK, Limulus polyphemus arginine kinase; PiTK, Phytophthora infestans taurocyamine kinase; TcCK, Tetronarce californica creatine kinase. (E) Fluorometric detection of amines in cellular extractions by HPLC/OPA. The control is a chromatogram of a standard mixture of amino acids and taurocyamine. Asp, aspartic acid; OH-Pro, hydroxyproline; P-ET-Amine, o-phosphoethanolamine; Pro, proline; P-Ser, o-phosphoserine; Ser, serine; Tac, taurocyamine; Tau, taurine; Thr, threonine; &#x3B1;-A-A-A, &#x3B1;-aminoadipic acid." class="highwire-fragment fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-1059355296" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;div xmlns=&quot;http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml&quot;&gt;Acquisition of the PK&#x2013;AMGT energy shuttle and buffering system by &lt;em&gt;C&lt;/em&gt;. &lt;em&gt;eustigma&lt;/em&gt; through HGT. The accession numbers of the respective amino acid sequences are indicated with the species names." class="highwire-fragment fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-1059355296" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;Phylogenetic relationship of AMGT proteins. The tree was constructed by the ML method (RaxML 8.0.0). ML BP &gt;50% obtained by RaxML and BI &gt;0.95 obtained by Bayesian analysis (MrBayes 3.2.6) are shown above the branches. The branch lengths reflect the evolutionary distances indicated by the scale bar. The accession numbers of the respective amino acid sequences are indicated with the species names.&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image lazyload" alt="Fig. S3." src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/114/39/E8304/F8.medium.gif" width="420" height="440"/><noscript><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image" alt="Fig. S3." src="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/114/39/E8304/F8.medium.gif" width="420" height="440"/></noscript></span></a></div></div><ul class="highwire-figure-links inline"><li class="download-fig first"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/114/39/E8304/F8.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download" title="Download Fig. S3." data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download figure</a></li>
<li class="new-tab"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/114/39/E8304/F8.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab" target="_blank" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Open in new tab</a></li>
<li class="download-ppt last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/27949" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download powerpoint</a></li>
</ul></div><div class="fig-caption"><span class="fig-label">Fig. S3.</span> <p id="p-37" class="first-child">Phylogenetic relationship of AMGT proteins. The tree was constructed by the ML method (RaxML 8.0.0). ML BP &gt;50% obtained by RaxML and BI &gt;0.95 obtained by Bayesian analysis (MrBayes 3.2.6) are shown above the branches. The branch lengths reflect the evolutionary distances indicated by the scale bar. The accession numbers of the respective amino acid sequences are indicated with the species names.</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div><p id="p-38">In the <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em> genome, PK1 and AMGT are encoded in the same scaffold close to each other, whereas PK2 is encoded in another scaffold (<a id="xref-fig-7-4" class="xref-fig" href="#F7">Fig. 5<em>A</em></a>). Phylogenetic analysis showed that <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em> PK1 and PK2 are most closely related to PK proteins of cryptophytes and stramenopiles, respectively (<a id="xref-fig-7-5" class="xref-fig" href="#F7">Fig. 5<em>B</em></a>). In addition, <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em> AMGT is most closely related to that of bacteria and cryptophytes (<a id="xref-fig-8-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F8">Fig. S3</a>). PK possesses a guanidine specificity region, which probably defines the substrate specificity (<a id="xref-ref-33-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-33">33</a>). To determine the substrate of <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em> PKs, the guanidine specificity region was compared with PKs of other organisms for which substrates have been determined. In the amino acid sequence alignment, the guanidine specificity regions of <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em> PK1 and PK2 were found to be most closely related to taurocyamine kinase (TK) of <em>Phytophthora infestans</em> (<a id="xref-fig-7-6" class="xref-fig" href="#F7">Fig. 5<em>D</em></a>). By HPLC/<em>o</em>-phthalaldehyde (OPA) fluorometry, taurocyamine was detected in <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em> cellular extract but not in that of <em>C</em>. <em>reinhardtii</em> (<a id="xref-fig-7-7" class="xref-fig" href="#F7">Fig. 5<em>E</em></a>). These results suggest that <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em> acquired TK and AMGT as an <span class="sc">l</span>-arginine:taurine amidinotransferase through HGT.</p><p id="p-39">The maintenance of a neutral cytosolic pH by acidophiles consumes a large amount of ATP, as described above (<a id="xref-ref-20-4" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-20">20</a>). It was previously shown that the “artificial HGT of PK,” that is, the expression of exogenous arginine kinase in yeasts and <em>Escherichia coli</em>, which do not possess endogenous PKs, increased the resistance to transient pH reduction by building up an energy-storing phospho-arginine pool (<a id="xref-ref-34-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-34">34</a>, <a id="xref-ref-35-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-35">35</a>). The RNA-seq results showed that <em>PK1</em>, <em>PK2</em>, and <em>AMGT</em> are relatively highly expressed in <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em>, exhibiting the 1,189th, 381th, and 1,050th highest RPKM values, respectively, among 14,105 protein-coding genes (<a id="xref-fig-5-3" class="xref-fig" href="#F5">Fig. S2</a> and <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1707072114/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1707072114.sd03.xlsx" class="in-nw">Dataset S3</a>). Thus, the acquisition of the PK–AMGT shuttle by <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em> has probably contributed to the supply of ATP needed to maintain cellular pH against an acidic environment (<a id="xref-fig-7-8" class="xref-fig" href="#F7">Fig. 5<em>C</em></a>).</p></div><div id="sec-7" class="subsection"><h3>Enhancement of Arsenic Biotransformation and Detoxification by <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em> Through HGT.</h3><p id="p-40">In addition to gene loss and acquisition through HGT, the genomic analysis of <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em> suggests that gene amplifications within the genome have also contributed to the adaptation to an acidic environment (<a id="xref-fig-9-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F9">Fig. 6<em>A</em></a>). It is known that natural acidic drainage often contains a very high concentration of toxic metals such as arsenic (<a id="xref-ref-36-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-36">36</a>). In addition to accelerating metal solubilization, acidic water protonates arsenic, which accelerates the penetration of arsenic into cells (<a id="xref-ref-2-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-2">2</a>). Arsenate (AsO<sub>4</sub><sup>3−</sup>), an analog of phosphate, is incorporated into cells along with phosphate, whereas arsenite (AsO<sub>3</sub><sup>3−</sup>) is incorporated into cells through aquaglycoporins (<a id="xref-fig-9-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F9">Fig. 6<em>B</em></a>) (<a id="xref-ref-37-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-37">37</a>). Arsenite oxidizes thiols of biomolecules and causes strong oxidative stress (<a id="xref-ref-38-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-38">38</a>). Consistent with the higher toxicity of arsenic in acidic environments, we found that <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em> tolerates a &gt;10 times higher concentration of arsenate than <em>C</em>. <em>reinhardtii</em> (<a id="xref-fig-9-3" class="xref-fig" href="#F9">Fig. 6<em>D</em></a>). Genomic analyses showed that genes involved in arsenic biotransformation and detoxification (<a id="xref-ref-37-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-37">37</a>) have been multiplied in the <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em> genome (<a id="xref-fig-9-4" class="xref-fig" href="#F9">Fig. 6<em>A</em></a>). The genome possesses approximately 10 copies of genes encoding arsenate reductase (ArsC) and arsenite efflux transporter (ACR3), which are located side-by-side in the genome, and approximately seven copies of the gene encoding arsenite S-adenosylmethionine methyltransferase (ArsM) (<a id="xref-fig-9-5" class="xref-fig" href="#F9">Fig. 6<em>A</em></a>). In addition, genes encoding glutaredoxin (Grx) (∼20 copies) and glutathione reductase (GR) (two copies), which are involved in the reduction of arsenate to arsenite (<a id="xref-ref-39-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-39">39</a>), have also been multiplied in the genome (<a id="xref-fig-9-6" class="xref-fig" href="#F9">Fig. 6<em>A</em></a>). Consistent with the increase in the gene copy number, RNA-seq analysis showed that <em>ArsM</em>, <em>Grx</em>, and <em>GR</em> mRNA levels are higher in <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em> than in <em>C</em>. <em>reinhardtii</em>, even when both are cultured under their respective optimal growth conditions without arsenic (<a id="xref-fig-5-4" class="xref-fig" href="#F5">Fig. S2</a> and <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1707072114/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1707072114.sd03.xlsx" class="in-nw">Datasets S3</a> and <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1707072114/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1707072114.sd04.xlsx" class="in-nw">S4</a>). In addition, <em>ArsC</em> and <em>ACR3</em> are also relatively highly expressed in <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em>, exhibiting the 1,001th and 177th highest RPKM, respectively, among 14,105 protein-coding genes (<a id="xref-fig-5-5" class="xref-fig" href="#F5">Fig. S2</a> and <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1707072114/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1707072114.sd03.xlsx" class="in-nw">Dataset S3</a>).</p><div id="F9" class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/114/39/E8304/F9.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Acquisition through HGT and expansion of genes in the arsenic biotransformation and detoxification system in C. eustigma. (A) Relative sequencing coverage ratio of Illumina MiSeq reads (200-bp window with 100-bp overlap) showing the copy numbers of arsenic biotransformation and detoxification genes. (B) Overview of the arsenic biotransformation and detoxification system (37). As(V) and As(III) are taken up through phosphate transporter (PTA) as a phosphate analog and through aquaglycoporins (AQP), respectively. As(V) is reduced to As(III) by glutaredoxin (Grx) by using glutathione (GSH) as a reductant or arsenate reductase ArsC or ACR2. As(III) is excreted by As(III)-specific transporter ArsA or ACR3. GSH is an antioxidant and is synthesized by &#x3B3;-glutamylcysteine synthetase (&#x3B3;-ECS) and glutathione synthetase (GSS). The methylation of As(III) to monomethylarsonic acid (MMA) and dimethylarsinic acid (DMA) is also thought to function as inorganic arsenic biotransformation and detoxification. As(III) is chelated by phytochelatins (PCs), which are synthesized by phytochelatin synthase (PCS) or GSH to form the As(III)-thiol complex, which is finally transported into the vacuole. (C) Presence or absence of arsenic biotransformation and detoxification genes in the genomes of five green algae (shown in green) and two thermo-acidophilic red algae (shown in red). The red and blue boxes indicate the presence of the gene; blue boxes indicate a gene that was horizontally transferred. The gray boxes indicate the absence of the gene. (D) Effects of the concentration of arsenate As(V) on the growth of C. eustigma and C. reinhardtii. Cells were cultured in modified original medium containing both 1 mM phosphate (originally 10 mM) and various concentrations of arsenate for 3 d. The error bars in the graph represent the SD of three biological replicates." class="highwire-fragment fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-1059355296" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;div xmlns=&quot;http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml&quot;&gt;Acquisition through HGT and expansion of genes in the arsenic biotransformation and detoxification system in &lt;em&gt;C&lt;/em&gt;. &lt;em&gt;eustigma&lt;/em&gt;. (&lt;em&gt;B&lt;/em&gt;) Overview of the arsenic biotransformation and detoxification system (37). As(V) and As(III) are taken up through phosphate transporter (PTA) as a phosphate analog and through aquaglycoporins (AQP), respectively. As(V) is reduced to As(III) by glutaredoxin (Grx) by using glutathione (GSH) as a reductant or arsenate reductase ArsC or ACR2. As(III) is excreted by As(III)-specific transporter ArsA or ACR3. GSH is an antioxidant and is synthesized by &#x3B3;-glutamylcysteine synthetase (&#x3B3;-ECS) and glutathione synthetase (GSS). The methylation of As(III) to monomethylarsonic acid (MMA) and dimethylarsinic acid (DMA) is also thought to function as inorganic arsenic biotransformation and detoxification. As(III) is chelated by phytochelatins (PCs), which are synthesized by phytochelatin synthase (PCS) or GSH to form the As(III)-thiol complex, which is finally transported into the vacuole. (&lt;em&gt;C&lt;/em&gt;) Presence or absence of arsenic biotransformation and detoxification genes in the genomes of five green algae (shown in green) and two thermo-acidophilic red algae (shown in red). The red and blue boxes indicate the presence of the gene; blue boxes indicate a gene that was horizontally transferred. The gray boxes indicate the absence of the gene. (&lt;em&gt;D&lt;/em&gt;) Effects of the concentration of arsenate As(V) on the growth of &lt;em&gt;C&lt;/em&gt;. &lt;em&gt;eustigma&lt;/em&gt; and &lt;em&gt;C&lt;/em&gt;. &lt;em&gt;reinhardtii&lt;/em&gt;.
