The error bars in the graph represent the SD of three biological replicates.&lt;/div&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image lazyload" alt="Fig. 6." src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/114/39/E8304/F9.medium.gif" width="377" height="440"/><noscript><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image" alt="Fig. 6." src="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/114/39/E8304/F9.medium.gif" width="377" height="440"/></noscript></span></a></div></div><ul class="highwire-figure-links inline"><li class="download-fig first"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/114/39/E8304/F9.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download" title="Download Fig. 6." data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download figure</a></li>
<li class="new-tab"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/114/39/E8304/F9.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab" target="_blank" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Open in new tab</a></li>
<li class="download-ppt last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/27951" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download powerpoint</a></li>
</ul></div><div class="fig-caption"><span class="fig-label">Fig. 6.</span> <p id="p-41" class="first-child">Acquisition through HGT and expansion of genes in the arsenic biotransformation and detoxification system in <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em>. (<em>A</em>) Relative sequencing coverage ratio of Illumina MiSeq reads (200-bp window with 100-bp overlap) showing the copy numbers of arsenic biotransformation and detoxification genes. (<em>B</em>) Overview of the arsenic biotransformation and detoxification system (<a id="xref-ref-37-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-37">37</a>). As(V) and As(III) are taken up through phosphate transporter (PTA) as a phosphate analog and through aquaglycoporins (AQP), respectively. As(V) is reduced to As(III) by glutaredoxin (Grx) by using glutathione (GSH) as a reductant or arsenate reductase ArsC or ACR2. As(III) is excreted by As(III)-specific transporter ArsA or ACR3. GSH is an antioxidant and is synthesized by γ-glutamylcysteine synthetase (γ-ECS) and glutathione synthetase (GSS). The methylation of As(III) to monomethylarsonic acid (MMA) and dimethylarsinic acid (DMA) is also thought to function as inorganic arsenic biotransformation and detoxification. As(III) is chelated by phytochelatins (PCs), which are synthesized by phytochelatin synthase (PCS) or GSH to form the As(III)-thiol complex, which is finally transported into the vacuole. (<em>C</em>) Presence or absence of arsenic biotransformation and detoxification genes in the genomes of five green algae (shown in green) and two thermo-acidophilic red algae (shown in red). The red and blue boxes indicate the presence of the gene; blue boxes indicate a gene that was horizontally transferred. The gray boxes indicate the absence of the gene. (<em>D</em>) Effects of the concentration of arsenate As(V) on the growth of <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em> and <em>C</em>. <em>reinhardtii</em>. Cells were cultured in modified original medium containing both 1 mM phosphate (originally 10 mM) and various concentrations of arsenate for 3 d. The error bars in the graph represent the SD of three biological replicates.</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div><p id="p-42">Several studies have already succeeded in enhancing the tolerance to arsenic by artificial HGT, for example, by overexpressing <em>E</em>. <em>coli</em> ArsC and γ-glutamylcysteine synthase (<a id="xref-ref-40-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-40">40</a>) (to increase the thiol pool) or overexpression of the yeast ACR3 in <em>Arabidopsis thaliana</em> (<a id="xref-ref-41-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-41">41</a>). Thus, the multiplication and high expression of arsenic biotransformation and detoxification genes in <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em> have probably contributed to the high algal resistance to arsenic.</p><p id="p-43">The comparison of green algal genomes showed that, among the proteins related to arsenic biotransformation and detoxification, ArsC and ACR3 are not encoded in other green algal genomes except for those of <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em> and <em>Co</em>. <em>subellipsoidea</em> (<a id="xref-fig-9-7" class="xref-fig" href="#F9">Fig. 6<em>C</em></a> and <a id="xref-table-wrap-5-1" class="xref-table" href="#T5">Table S5</a>). Based on the phylogenetic relationship between these two species, <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em> and <em>Co</em>. <em>subellipsoidea</em> probably acquired <em>ArsC</em> and <em>ACR3</em> genes independently (<a id="xref-fig-9-8" class="xref-fig" href="#F9">Fig. 6<em>C</em></a>). In the phylogenetic analyses, ArsC of <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em> and <em>C</em>. <em>subellipsoidea</em> formed a clade with those of acidobacteria, actinobacteria, and δ-proteobacteria (<a id="xref-fig-10-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F10">Fig. S4</a>), suggesting the bacterial HGT origin of <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em> ArsC. On the other hand, <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em> and <em>Co</em>. <em>subellipsoidea</em> ACR3 formed a clade with proteins of charophycean algae and certain land plant species, and this clade is a sister group of fungal proteins (<a id="xref-fig-11-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F11">Fig. S5</a>). Thus, the origin of <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em> ACR3 is not clear at this point; however, given that only a limited number of green algae and land plants possess <em>ACR3</em> (<a id="xref-fig-11-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F11">Fig. S5</a>), it is likely that <em>ACR3</em> was acquired by these species multiple times independently through HGT. Thus, the multiplication of both genes derived from their eukaryotic ancestor (<em>ACR3</em>, <em>Grx</em>, and <em>GR</em>) and genes acquired through HGT (<em>ArsC</em> and <em>ACR3</em>) probably contributed to the adaptation of <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em>.</p><div id="T5" class="table pos-float type-supplementary-material"><div class="table-inline table-callout-links"><div class="callout"><span>View this table:</span><ul class="callout-links"><li class="view-inline first"><a href="##" class="table-expand-inline" data-table-url="/highwire/markup/27954/expansion?postprocessors=highwire_tables%2Chighwire_reclass%2Chighwire_figures%2Chighwire_math%2Chighwire_inline_linked_media%2Chighwire_embed&amp;table-expand-inline=1" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">View inline</a></li>
<li class="view-popup last"><a href="/highwire/markup/27954/expansion?width=1000&amp;height=500&amp;iframe=true&amp;postprocessors=highwire_tables%2Chighwire_reclass%2Chighwire_figures%2Chighwire_math%2Chighwire_inline_linked_media%2Chighwire_embed" class="colorbox colorbox-load table-expand-popup" rel="gallery-fragment-tables" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">View popup</a></li>

</ul></div><div class="fig-caption"><span class="fig-label">Fig. S5.</span> <p id="p-46" class="first-child">Phylogenetic relationship of ACR3 proteins. The tree was constructed by the ML method (RaxML 8.0.0). ML BP &gt;50% obtained by RaxML and BI &gt;0.95 obtained by Bayesian analysis (MrBayes 3.2.6) are shown above the branches. The branch lengths reflect the evolutionary distances indicated by the scale bar. The accession numbers of the respective amino acid sequences are indicated with the species names.</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div></div></div><div class="section discussion" id="sec-8"><h2 class="">Discussion</h2><p id="p-47" class="flushleft">The above analyses showed that the <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em> genome has experienced large-scale duplication throughout its genome (<a id="xref-fig-2-8" class="xref-fig" href="#F2">Fig. 2<em>B</em></a>) and has a lower GC content than evolutionarily related neutrophilic green algae sequenced thus far (<a id="xref-fig-2-9" class="xref-fig" href="#F2">Fig. 2<em>C</em></a>). However, it is currently unclear whether there are any relationships between these features in the genome structure and the adaptation to an acidic environment. Generally, genome or gene duplication is widely considered to facilitate environmental adaptation because the redundancy generated allows the evolution of new beneficial gene functions that are otherwise prohibited due to functional constraints (<a id="xref-ref-42-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-42">42</a>). Genomic GC content is predicted to affect genome functioning and species ecology significantly. However, the biological significance of GC content diversity remains elusive because of a lack of sufficiently robust genomic data (<a id="xref-ref-43-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-43">43</a>).</p><p id="p-48">Comparative genome and transcriptome analyses suggest that the following features of genomic evolution have contributed to the adaptation of <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em> to an acidic environment. (<em>i</em>) <em>HSP</em>s and <em>PMA</em> became expressed at high levels. (<em>ii</em>) The genome lost fermentative genes that produce organic acids in the cell. (<em>iii</em>) The genome acquired genes encoding the PK–AMGT energy shuttle and buffering system and genes that are involved in arsenic biotransformation and detoxification through HGT. (<em>iv</em>) The genes involved in arsenic biotransformation and detoxification, derived from a green algal ancestor or acquired through HGT, were multiplied in the genome.</p><p id="p-49">In addition, based on this study and the results of previous studies in other acidophilic algae, it is suggested that these genomic changes are probably common trends in the adaptation to acidic or other extreme environments, as discussed below. Regarding (<em>i</em>), we also found that <em>HSP</em>s are highly expressed in the thermo-acidophilic red alga <em>Cy</em><strong>.</strong> <em>merolae</em> under its optimal conditions (in an autotrophic medium at pH 2.5 and 42 °C), as in the case of <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em> compared with <em>C</em>. <em>reinhardtii</em> (<a id="xref-fig-5-6" class="xref-fig" href="#F5">Fig. S2</a> and <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1707072114/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1707072114.sd05.xlsx" class="in-nw">Dataset S5</a>). Regarding (<em>ii</em>), <em>Co</em>. <em>subellipsoidea</em> also lost organic acid-producing fermentation genes independently from <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em> (<a id="xref-fig-6-15" class="xref-fig" href="#F6">Fig. 4 <em>A</em> and <em>B</em></a>). The genus <em>Coccomyxa</em> contains several acidophiles (<a id="xref-ref-29-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-29">29</a>, <a id="xref-ref-30-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-30">30</a>). However, it is currently unclear whether there is a correlation between the loss of organic acid-producing fermentation and adaptation to an acidic environment in acidophilic red algae, because they possess only the lactate fermentation pathway and alcohol dehydrogenases (<a id="xref-fig-6-16" class="xref-fig" href="#F6">Fig. 4 <em>A</em> and <em>B</em></a>). Regarding (<em>iii</em>), although the HGT of PK–AMGT into acidophiles has not been reported in other acidophiles, the acquisition of arsenic biotransformation and detoxification genes through HGT has been found in the green alga <em>Co</em>. <em>subellipsoidea</em> and thermo-acidophilic red algae (<a id="xref-fig-9-9" class="xref-fig" href="#F9">Fig. 6<em>C</em></a>) (<a id="xref-ref-8-5" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-8">8</a>). The green alga <em>Co</em>. <em>subellipsoidea</em> acquired ACR3 (<a id="xref-fig-9-10" class="xref-fig" href="#F9">Fig. 6<em>C</em></a> and <a id="xref-fig-11-3" class="xref-fig" href="#F11">Fig. S5</a>), and the red alga <em>G</em>. <em>sulphuraria</em> acquired ArsB through HGT (<a id="xref-ref-8-6" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-8">8</a>) (<a id="xref-fig-9-11" class="xref-fig" href="#F9">Fig. 6<em>C</em></a>). Regarding (<em>iv</em>), in the <em>G</em>. <em>sulphuraria</em> genome, genes of the chloride channel and chloride carrier/channel families have been multiplied and are thought to be important to acid tolerance (<a id="xref-ref-8-7" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-8">8</a>). In addition, an archaeal ATPase of HGT origin has been multiplied and probably contributes to heat tolerance (<a id="xref-ref-8-8" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-8">8</a>).</p><p id="p-50">This study and recent studies on the genomes of acidophiles have started to reveal commonalities in genomic evolution regarding adaptation to an acidic environment. Besides increasing our understanding of evolution, this information could also have important applications. Microalgae have been cultivated at a large scale to produce functional foods and pigments and are also considered to be an alternative source for biofuels because of their relatively rapid growth to a high concentration (<a id="xref-ref-44-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-44">44</a>). Acidophilic microalgae have an advantage in that they can be cultivated outdoors without the risk of contamination by other undesirable organisms (<a id="xref-ref-45-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-45">45</a>). In addition, trials using acidophiles for bioremediation (<a id="xref-ref-46-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-46">46</a>) and metal recovery have also been initiated (<a id="xref-ref-47-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-47">47</a>). An understanding of the genetic basis of acid and/or metal tolerance is also necessary to confer such abilities to other organisms.</p></div><div class="section methods" id="sec-9"><h2 class="">Methods</h2><div id="sec-10" class="subsection"><h3>Algal Strains.</h3><p id="p-51"><em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em> (NIES-2499; Microbial Culture Collection at the National Institute for Environmental Studies) and <em>C</em>. <em>reinhardtii</em> 137c mt+ were used in the current study. <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em> and <em>C</em>. <em>reinhardtii</em> were maintained with gyration (100 rpm) on a rotary shaker (NR-2; Taitec) in photoautotrophic medium (9.35 mM NH<sub>4</sub>Cl, 81.15 μM MgSO<sub>4</sub>·7H<sub>2</sub>O, 68.04 μM CaCl<sub>2</sub>, 10 mM KH<sub>2</sub>PO<sub>4</sub>/K<sub>2</sub>HPO<sub>4</sub>, 59.2 μM FeCl<sub>3</sub>, 0.73 μM MnCl<sub>2</sub>·4H<sub>2</sub>O, 0.31 μM ZnSO<sub>4</sub>·7H<sub>2</sub>O, 0.0672 μM CoCl<sub>2</sub>·6H<sub>2</sub>O, 0.0413 μM Na<sub>2</sub>MoO<sub>4</sub>·2H<sub>2</sub>O, 0.5046 μM CuSO<sub>4</sub>·5H<sub>2</sub>O, 13.75 μM Na<sub>2</sub>EDTA·2H<sub>2</sub>O) at pH 3.0 and pH 7.0, respectively, at 21 °C under a 12-h light/12-h dark photoperiod (30 μE⋅m<sup>−2</sup>⋅s<sup>−1</sup>).</p></div><div id="sec-11" class="subsection"><h3>Genomic DNA Sequencing.</h3><p id="p-52">Genomic DNA of <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em> was extracted, and sequencing libraries were prepared according to ref. <a id="xref-ref-48-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-48">48</a>. The shotgun and paired-end libraries (8 kb) were sequenced by Roche 454 GS FLX+ Titanium (Roche Diagnostics). The paired-end (400-bp) library was sequenced by HiSeq 2500 with 100 base-paired end format with the TruSeq SBS kit v3 (Illumina, Inc.). The paired-end (800-bp) and mate-pair libraries (3, 5, and 8 kb) were sequenced by MiSeq (Illumina, Inc.) with the MiSeq reagent kit version 3 (600 cycles; Illumina). The MiSeq reads were filtered using ShortReadManager (<a id="xref-ref-49-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-49">49</a>), based on a 17-mer frequency.</p></div><div id="sec-12" class="subsection"><h3>Estimation of the Genome Size by K-mer Analysis.</h3><p id="p-53">The MiSeq paired-end reads were used for the K-mer 31 frequency distribution analysis using JELLYFISH (<a id="xref-ref-50-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-50">50</a>). The total genome size was estimated by analyzing the occurrence and distribution of K-mers using the following formula: Estimated genome size in base pairs = K-mer number/depth. The 31 K-mer depth distribution of the MiSeq paired-end reads exhibited two peaks (<a id="xref-fig-2-10" class="xref-fig" href="#F2">Fig. 2<em>A</em></a>). The estimated genome size of <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em> was ∼130 Mb, when the ×19 was considered as the main peak.</p></div><div id="sec-13" class="subsection"><h3>Genome Assembly, Scaffolding, and Gap Closing.</h3><p id="p-54">The Roche 454 shotgun and paired-end reads were assembled de novo by Newbler version 2.9 (Roche) with the following parameters: -mi 98 -mL 80 -scaffold -large -s 500. Subsequent scaffolding of the Newbler output contigs was performed by SSPACE (<a id="xref-ref-51-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-51">51</a>) using the Illumina paired-end and mate-pair information (<a id="xref-table-wrap-2-3" class="xref-table" href="#T2">Table S2</a>). GMcloser (<a id="xref-ref-52-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-52">52</a>) was used for gap filling with preassembled contigs and Illumina paired-end reads. The genome sequence was improved with Illumina paired-end reads using iCORN2 (<a id="xref-ref-53-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-53">53</a>). To remove mitochondrial and chloroplast DNA sequences, tblastn (<a id="xref-ref-54-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-54">54</a>) searches were performed against scaffolds by using amino acid sequences of <em>C</em>. <em>reinhardtii</em> mitochondrion- and chloroplast-encoded proteins as queries. By the tblastn search, one mitochondrial DNA scaffold and two chloroplast DNA scaffolds were identified, and these scaffolds were removed from the assembly.</p></div><div id="sec-14" class="subsection"><h3>Estimation of Coverage Ratio in the <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em> Genome.</h3><p id="p-55">The sequencing coverage ratio was assessed by calculating normalized coverage depth followed by manual inspection of depth variation along each scaffold. The MiSeq read data (6,660,746 reads) were mapped to the scaffolds using Bowtie2 versiom 2.1.0 (<a id="xref-ref-55-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-55">55</a>) with default settings. Mapped reads in sequence alignment/map (SAM) format were converted to the binary version of the SAM file (BAM format) using the Samtools version 0.1.19 (<a id="xref-ref-56-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-56">56</a>) &lt;view&gt;, &lt;sort&gt;, and &lt;index&gt; commands. The aligned reads in BAM format were filtered for duplicates using the Samtools version 0.1.19 &lt;rmdup&gt; command. After the removal of duplicates, BAM files were converted to browser extensible data (BED) format using the Bedtools version 2.17 (<a id="xref-ref-57-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-57">57</a>) &lt;bamToBed&gt; command. Genome-wide windows were defined using the Bedtools &lt;makewindows&gt; command, and then coverage depth of each individual window was calculated using the Bedtools &lt;coverage&gt; command. The histogram of the coverage ratio exhibited two major peaks that probably correspond to single and duplicated regions. The relative coverage ratio (shown in <a id="xref-fig-2-11" class="xref-fig" href="#F2">Figs. 2<em>B</em></a> and <a id="xref-fig-9-12" class="xref-fig" href="#F9">6<em>A</em></a>) was normalized by the averaged coverage depth of the probable single regions.</p></div><div id="sec-15" class="subsection"><h3>Prediction and Annotation of the Nuclear Genes.</h3><p id="p-56">Nuclear genes were predicted by Augustus 3.0.3 (<a id="xref-ref-58-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-58">58</a>). Assembled transcript sequences were mapped to the scaffolds by BLAT (<a id="xref-ref-59-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-59">59</a>) to assess the likelihood that each sequence was indeed a transcript. The manually curated 1,900 gene models were used as Augustus training sets, and 14,105 genes were predicted by Augustus with transcript evidence. The KEGG Orthology ID assignment was performed for all predicted genes in the <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em> and <em>C</em>. <em>reinhardtii</em> genomes. The assignments were performed by the KAAS (<a id="xref-ref-60-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-60">60</a>).</p></div><div id="sec-16" class="subsection"><h3>Comparison of mRNA Levels of Orthologous Genes in <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em> and <em>C</em>. <em>reinhardtii</em>.</h3><p id="p-57">To identify one-to-one orthologous genes in the three Volvocales (<em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em>, <em>C</em>. <em>reinhardtii</em>, and <em>V</em>. <em>carteri</em>), gene clustering analysis was performed by OrthoMCL (<a id="xref-ref-61-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-61">61</a>) with the following parameters: inflation value = 1.5, percentMatchCutoff = 50, and evalueExponentCutoff = −10. A BLASTP search for predicted amino acid sequences with an E-value of 1e<sup>−10</sup> in the three algal species was performed using NCBI BLAST+ version 2.2.30. In total, we found 4,590 one-to-one ortholog pairs. Gene-expression scores were obtained from RNA-seq data by mapping the clean reads to the genes by Bowtie2 version 2.1.0 (<a id="xref-ref-55-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-55">55</a>). SAMtools (<a id="xref-ref-56-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-56">56</a>), BEDtools (<a id="xref-ref-57-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-57">57</a>), and R version 2.14.2 (<a id="xref-ref-62-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-62">62</a>) were used to calculate the tag-count data that were mapped to the coding genes. Normalization of the orthologous gene-expression scores was performed by RPKM normalization. After obtaining the normalized expression scores of orthologous genes for each sample, the scores were log (base 10)-transformed and plotted to produce a scatter graph for comparison of the expression scores of the two algal species.</p></div><div id="sec-17" class="subsection"><h3>Comparison of mRNA Levels in Algal Species.</h3><p id="p-58">The RNA-seq reads were mapped to the <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em>, <em>C</em>. <em>reinhardtii</em> (JGI, version 5.5), or <em>Cy</em>. <em>merolae</em> (<em>Cyanidioschyzon merolae</em> Genome Project) coding sequences by Bowtie2 (<a id="xref-ref-55-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-55">55</a>) with the default parameters. The Bowtie2 outputs were processed to obtain tag counts. Since it has been shown that the GC content affects the read abundances in an RNA-seq dataset (<a id="xref-ref-63-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-63">63</a>), counts were full-quantile normalized within a sample by GC content bias-correction methods implemented in the EDASeq R package (<a id="xref-ref-64-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-64">64</a>). These normalized counts were used to calculate the mRNA level of each gene (in RPKM units) in the algal samples according to ref. <a id="xref-ref-65-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-65">65</a>.</p></div><div id="sec-18" class="subsection"><h3>Data Availability.</h3><p id="p-59">The <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em> NIES-2499 whole-genome and gene models have been deposited in DNA Data Bank of Japan (DDBJ)/European Molecular Biology Laboratory (EMBL)/GenBank under the accession code PRJDB5468. The dataset includes sequences of the nuclear and mitochondrial genomes. Because the chloroplast genome was highly repetitive and the genome could not be assembled well, the chloroplast genome was omitted from the dataset. The RNA-seq data of <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em> and <em>C</em>. <em>reinhardtii</em> have been deposited in DDBJ/EMBL/GenBank (accession codes PRJDB6154 and PRJDB6155, respectively).</p></div></div><div class="section supplementary-material" id="sec-19"><h2 class="">SI Methods</h2><div id="sec-20" class="subsection"><h3>Determination of Optimal pH Conditions.</h3><p id="p-60">To determine the optimal pH for <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em> and <em>C</em>. <em>reinhardtii</em>, cells were cultured at 20 °C in photoautotrophic medium buffered at eight different pH values (pH 1.0–8.0). The medium was buffered with 20 mM of the chemicals indicated below, except for the pH 1.0, 2.0, and 3.0 media (the pH levels of these media were adjusted with 1 N H<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub>/1 N KOH). The chemicals used to buffer the pH were 3,3-dimethylglutaric acid (DMGA) for pH 4.0, 2-<em>N</em>-morpholino)ethanesulfonic acid (Mes) for pH 5.0, piperazine-<em>N</em>,<em>N</em>′-bis (Pipes) for pH 6.0, 3-<em>N</em>-morpholino)propanesulfonic acid (Mops) for pH 7.0, and Hepes for pH 8.0. <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em> and <em>C</em>. <em>reinhardtii</em> were cultured at pH 3.0 and pH 7.0, respectively, until they reached an OD<sub>750</sub> of 1.0–2.0. Then, the cells were harvested by centrifugation at 1,500 × <em>g</em> for 5 min and were gently resuspended into 50 mL of each medium (from pH 1.0 to pH 8.0) to give an OD<sub>750</sub> of 0.5. Cells were cultured at 20 °C in 100-mL test tubes (∼3 cm thick) under continuous light (90 µE⋅m<sup>−2</sup>⋅s<sup>−1</sup>) with aeration (0.3 L ambient air/min) for 24 h. OD<sub>750</sub> was measured with a spectrophotometer (SmartSpec Plus; Bio-Rad). Growth rates were determined according to the method described in ref. <a id="xref-ref-68-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-68">68</a>.</p></div><div id="sec-21" class="subsection"><h3>Reidentification of <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em> in the AMD and Quality Analyses of the AMD Water.</h3><p id="p-61"><em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em> (NIES-2499) was originally isolated together with acidophilic mosses from AMD of an abandoned sulfur mine in Nagano Prefecture, Japan (<a id="xref-ref-14-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-14">14</a>). In September 2013, we visited the same AMD (pH 2.13, 14.5 °C), which is located in Joshinetsu-kogen National Park, Japan. We harvested the mosses from the AMD with administrators of Hokushin Forest Office, which is responsible for management of the forest. The moss samples, to which algae adhere, were incubated in M-Allen liquid medium (<a id="xref-ref-69-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-69">69</a>) at pH 2.0 and 15 °C under continuous-light conditions (20 µE⋅m<sup>2</sup>⋅s<sup>−1</sup>). The algae in the liquid culture were serially diluted and spread on M-Allen agar medium. After incubation at 20 °C under continuous-light conditions (20 µE⋅m<sup>2</sup>⋅s<sup>−1</sup>), green colonies appeared. As determined by microscopy, the cells in most of these colonies exhibited <em>Chlamydomonas</em>-like morphology. The 18S rDNA of the alga was also amplified by PCR and sequenced as described previously (<a id="xref-ref-70-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-70">70</a>). The ∼1.5-kb sequence of 18S rDNA of the isolated alga was completely identical to that of <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em> (NIES-2499).</p><p id="p-62">The nutrient contents of the AMD water were determined according to ref. <a id="xref-ref-71-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-71">71</a> as follows. Fe<sup>2+</sup> and Fe<sup>3+</sup> were quantified by the 1,10-phenanthroline photometric method. Al<sup>3+</sup>, Cu<sup>2+</sup>, and Zn<sup>2+</sup> were quantified by inductively coupled plasma mass spectrometry. Mn<sup>2+</sup>, Na<sup>+</sup>, K<sup>+</sup>, Mg<sup>2+</sup>, and Ca<sup>2+</sup> were quantified by atomic absorption spectrometry. SO<sub>4</sub><sup>2−</sup> was quantified by the barium sulfate turbidimetric method. Cl<sup>−</sup> was quantified by Mohr’s method. NH<sup>4+</sup> was quantified by the indophenol method. PO<sub>4</sub><sup>3−</sup> was quantified by the molybdenum blue colorimetric method. H<sub>2</sub>SiO<sub>3</sub> was quantified by the molybdenum yellow colorimetric method.</p></div><div id="sec-22" class="subsection"><h3>Characterization of Arsenate Tolerance.</h3><p id="p-63">The comparison of arsenate tolerance was determined as described in ref. <a id="xref-ref-72-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-72">72</a>, with minor modifications. <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em> and <em>C</em>. <em>reinhardtii</em> cells cultured in the medium at pH 3.0 and at pH 7.0, respectively, and 20 °C (OD<sub>750</sub> of 1.0–2.0) were harvested by centrifugation at 1,500 × <em>g</em> for 5 min and then were gently resuspended in the same fresh medium supplemented with 1 mM phosphate and a series of concentrations of potassium arsenate (KH<sub>2</sub>AsO<sub>4</sub>), as indicated in <a id="xref-fig-9-13" class="xref-fig" href="#F9">Fig. 6<em>D</em></a>, to give an OD<sub>750</sub> of 0.5. Cells were cultured with gyration in a 24-well culture plate at 20 °C under continuous light (100⋅μE⋅m<sup>−2</sup>⋅s<sup>−1</sup>) for 3 d.</p></div><div id="sec-23" class="subsection"><h3>RNA-Seq Analyses.</h3><p id="p-64"><em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em> and <em>C</em>. <em>reinhardtii</em> were cultured in 50 mL of photoautotrophic medium at pH 3.0 and pH 7.0, respectively, in 100-mL test tubes (∼3 cm thick) at 20 °C under continuous-light conditions (100 μE⋅m<sup>−2</sup>⋅s<sup>−1</sup>) with aeration (0.3 L ambient air/min). Total RNA was extracted from 10 mL of log-phase culture (∼1.0 × 10<sup>6</sup> cells/mL) of each of <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em> and <em>C</em>. <em>reinhardtii</em>. The unicellular red alga <em>Cy</em>. <em>merolae</em> was cultured in 50 mL of 2× Allen’s medium (a photoautotrophic medium) at pH 2.5 in 100-mL test tubes (∼3 cm thick) and 42 °C under continuous-light conditions (100 μE⋅m<sup>−2</sup>⋅s<sup>−1</sup>) with aeration (0.3 L ambient air/min). Total RNA was extracted from 20 mL of log-phase culture (∼1.0 × 10<sup>7</sup> cells/mL) of <em>Cy</em>. <em>merolae</em>. Cell numbers were determined using an improved Neubauer hemacytometer.</p><p id="p-65">mRNA of respective samples was purified from 10 μg of total RNA with Dynabeads Oligo(dT)25 (Life Technologies), according to ref. <a id="xref-ref-73-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-73">73</a>. Sequencing libraries were prepared according to ref. <a id="xref-ref-74-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-74">74</a>. Sequencing was performed by HiSeq 2500 with 100 base-paired end format with TruSeq SBS kit v3 (Illumina). The reads were cleaned up using the cutadapt program version 1.81 (<a id="xref-ref-75-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-75">75</a>) by trimming low-quality ends (&lt;QV30) and adapter sequences and by discarding reads shorter than 50 bp.</p></div><div id="sec-24" class="subsection"><h3>Molecular Phylogenetic Analyses.</h3><p id="p-66">The nucleic acid (23S rDNA, 16S rDNA, atpB, psaA, psaB, psbC, and rbcL) or amino acid (TK, AMGT, ArsC, and ACR3 proteins) sequences were aligned using MAFFT version 7.212 (<a id="xref-ref-76-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-76">76</a>), and ambiguous sites were excluded by Gblocks version 0.91b (<a id="xref-ref-77-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-77">77</a>). Concatenation of the datasets (chloroplast 16S rDNA, 23S rDNA, atpB, psaA, psaB, psbC, and rbcL) was performed with Kakusan4 (<a id="xref-ref-78-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-78">78</a>). Substitution models were selected by Kakusan4 or Aminosan (<a id="xref-ref-78-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-78">78</a>) (GTR+G for 16S rDNA, 23S rDNA, atpB, psaA, psaB, psbC, and rbcL; LG+G for TK, AMGT, and ArsC proteins; LG+F+G for ACR3 protein). The ML analysis and 1,000 pseudoreplicates of bootstrap analyses were performed by RaxML 8.0.0 (<a id="xref-ref-79-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-79">79</a>). Bayesian analyses were performed by MrBayes 3.2.6 (<a id="xref-ref-80-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-80">80</a>) using the optimal substitution models with 1 million iterations, a subsample frequency of 1,000, and a burn-in of 100,000. The ML and Bayesian analyses of the concatenated datasets (21 taxa, 11,367 sites) were calculated under separate model conditions.</p></div><div id="sec-25" class="subsection"><h3>Quantification of Extracellular Metabolites by HPLC/Gas Chromatography.</h3><p id="p-67"><em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em> and <em>C</em>. <em>reinhardtii</em> cells cultured in the media at pH 3.0 and pH 7.0, respectively, and 20 °C (OD<sub>750</sub> of 1.0–2.0) were harvested by centrifugation at 1,500 × <em>g</em> for 5 min and then were gently resuspended into 50 mL of respective fresh medium to give an OD<sub>750</sub> of 0.5. These cells were then cultured at 20 °C under continuous light (100 μE⋅m<sup>2</sup>⋅s<sup>−1</sup>) with aeration (0.25 L ambient air/min) for 2 d and then were continuously flushed with N<sub>2</sub> gas in the dark. <em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em> and <em>C</em>. <em>reinhardtii</em> cells were harvested by centrifugation at 1,500 × <em>g</em> for 5 min from respective 10-mL cultures just before and 4 h after the onset of aeration with N<sub>2</sub> in the dark. The supernatant was transferred to a new vial, and the sample was then frozen in liquid N<sub>2</sub> for subsequent analyses. Cellular dry weights were measured according to the method described in ref. <a id="xref-ref-68-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-68">68</a>. Organic acid analysis was performed by HPLC (Shimadzu Prominence; Shimadzu). The supernatant sample was thawed, centrifuged, and filtered (pore size of 0.22 μm), and then 50 μL of the sample was injected using an autosampler (SIL-20AC; Shimadzu). The sample was separated with two serial ion-exclusion chromatography columns (Shim-pack SCR-102H; Shimadzu) equipped with a guard column (Shim-pack SCR-102HG; Shimadzu) at 45 °C in a mobile phase of 5 mM p-toluenesulfonic acid, and then the eluent pH was adjusted with a buffering solution containing 5 mM p-toluenesulfonic acid, 20 mM Bis-Tris, and 0.1 mM EDTA at a flow rate of 0.8 mL/min. Each acid was quantified with a conductivity detector (CDD-10AVP; Shimadzu). Retention peaks for the various organic acids were recorded by LabSolutions LC/gas chromatography software, and quantification was performed by comparisons with the absorption of known amounts of a standard for respective organic acids. Ethanol analysis was performed by gas chromatography with an FID detector (GC-2014; Shimadzu) using a glass column of Thermon-1000 5% Sunpak-A (Shimadzu). The column temperature was maintained at 130 °C, and the temperatures of the injector and detector were maintained at 180 °C. N<sub>2</sub> was used as a carrier gas at a flow rate of 50 mL/min. Aliquots (1.0 μL) of samples were injected using an autosampler (AOC-20c; Shimadzu).</p></div><div id="sec-26" class="subsection"><h3>Detection of Taurocyamine by HPLC/OPA Fluorometry.</h3><p id="p-68"><em>C</em>. <em>eustigma</em> and <em>C</em>. <em>reinhardtii</em> cells cultured in medium at pH 3.0 and pH 7.0, respectively, and 20 °C (OD<sub>750</sub> of ∼1.0) were harvested by centrifugation at 1,500 × <em>g</em> for 5 min and then were gently resuspended into 400 mL of respective fresh medium to give an OD<sub>750</sub> of 0.5. Cells were cultured in 700-mL flat bottles (∼5 cm thick) at 20 °C under continuous-light conditions (100 μE⋅m<sup>−2</sup>⋅s<sup>−1</sup>) with aeration (1.0 L ambient air/min) for 1 d. They were then harvested from the respective 50-mL cultures by centrifugation at 1,500 × <em>g</em> for 5 min and were stored at −80 °C until use. Taurocyamine was extracted from the cells as described by ref. <a id="xref-ref-81-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-81">81</a>, with minor modifications. Briefly, taurocyamine was extracted from the cell pellets twice with 1 mL of ice-cold 80% ethanol and then was centrifuged (16,100 × <em>g</em> for 5 min at 4 °C). The supernatants (2 mL) were extracted with 2 mL of ice-cold chloroform to remove pigments and fatty materials. The aqueous fraction was dried by a vacuum freeze dryer (EYELA, FRD-1200; Rikakikai Co. Ltd.). The dried sample was then dissolved in 100 µL of sodium citrate buffer (pH 2.2) solution (Wako Pure Chemical Industries). Analysis of amines was performed by HPLC (Shimadzu Prominence; Shimadzu) with postcolumn fluorescence derivatization using OPA/<em>N</em>-acetylcysteine. Then, 10-μL aliquots of the samples were injected using an autosampler (SIL-20AC; Shimadzu). Samples were separated with an ion-exclusion chromatography column (Shim-pack Amino-Na; Shimadzu) using the Amino Acid Mobile Phase Kit (Na Type; Shimadzu) with gradient elution according to the manufacturer’s protocol (flow rate, 0.4 mL/min; column temperature, 60 °C). A trap column (ISC-30/S0504 Na; Shimadzu) was placed between the pump and the autosampler to trap ammonia. The postcolumn fluorescence derivatization was conducted with an Amino Acid Reaction Reagent Kit (Shimadzu) according to the manufacturer’s protocol (flow rate, 0.2 mL/min; reaction temperature, 60 °C). Derivatized amines were monitored by a fluorescence detector (RF-20Axs; Shimadzu) with 350-nm excitation and 450-nm emission. Retention peaks for the various amines were recorded by LabSolutions LC/gas chromatography software.</p></div></div><div class="section ack" id="ack-1"><h2>Acknowledgments</h2><p id="p-69">We thank Dr. T. Kuroiwa, Dr. H. Kuroiwa, Dr. K. Tanaka, and Dr. Y. Kabeya for kind encouragement and advice on this work; Dr. K. Hori, Dr. N. V. Sasaki, Dr. M. Ishikawa, Dr. T. Fujisawa, and Mr. Y. Kazama for advice on bioinformatic analyses; Dr. U. Goodenough and Dr. Y. Nishimura for advice on <em>Chlamydomonas</em> research; Mr. T. Sone for advice on RNA extraction; and members of the S.-y.M. Laboratory for their technical advice and support. This work was supported by the Core Research for Evolutional Science and Technology Program of the Japan Science and Technology Agency (S.-y.M.), by Grant-in-Aid for Scientific Research from the Japan Society for the Promotion of Science 25251039 (to S.-y.M.), and by the Ministry of Education, Culture, Sports, Science and Technology-supported Program for Strategic Research Foundation at Private Universities 2013–2017 S1311017. Computations were partially performed on the National Institute of Genetics (NIG) supercomputer at the Research Organization of Information and Systems of the NIG.</p></div><div class="section fn-group" id="fn-group-1"><h2>Footnotes</h2><ul class="pnas-footnotes"><li class="corresp" id="corresp-1"><a class="rev-xref" href="#xref-corresp-1-1">↵</a><sup>1</sup>To whom correspondence may be addressed.
